### Question 1: CLAIMS-1

**Query:** How do I file a VA disability claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 11.35s

**Answer:**

To file a VA disability claim, you have several options:

- File online by yourself at VA.gov: [https://www.va.gov/disability/how-to-file-claim/](https://www.va.gov/disability/how-to-file-claim/) or directly file the VA Form 21-526EZ online at [https://www.va.gov/disability/file-disability-claim-form-21-526ez/](https://www.va.gov/disability/file-disability-claim-form-21-526ez/)³⁶.
- Use a Veterans Service Officer (VSO) to assist you with filing. VSOs can help you prepare and submit your claim properly. You can find a VSO near you at [https://www.va.gov/ogc/apps/accreditation/index.asp](https://www.va.gov/ogc/apps/accreditation/index.asp)¹³.
- File by fax: send VA Form 21-526EZ to 844-531-7818³.
- File in person: complete VA Form 21-526EZ and submit it at your nearest VA Regional Office³.
- File by mail: send the completed VA Form 21-526EZ to the Department of Veterans Affairs Claims Intake Center, PO Box 4444, Janesville, WI 53547-4444³.

Important notes:
- If you have a VSO file your claim, you must personally sign the claim paperwork if this is your first claim².
- You do not need to have a diagnosis to file a claim².
- The VA processes claims based on the date they are received and processed, not the postmark date³.
- If you file a new claim while another is active, the VA will close the new claim but combine the issues with the active claim³.

It is strongly recommended to use a VSO to help with the process, although you can file on your own if you prefer⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing a VA Disability Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Filing a BDD Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
³ [Filing a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁴ [How to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
⁶ [How do I file a claim for disability compensation?](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#howdoifileaclaimfordisabilitycompensation)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Filing a VA Disability Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 2: CLAIMS-2

**Query:** What forms do I need to file a claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 7.81s

**Answer:**

To file a VA disability claim, the primary form you need is:

- VA Form 21-526EZ, "Application for Disability Compensation and Related Compensation Benefits"¹

If you are filing other types of claims, such as Dependency and Indemnity Compensation or accrued benefits, you may need:

- VA Form 21P-534EZ, "Application for Dependency and Indemnity Compensation, Death Pension and Accrued Benefits"  
- VA Form 21P-535, "Application for Dependency and Indemnity Compensation by Parent(s)"  
- VA Form 21P-601, "Application for Accrued Amounts Due a Deceased Beneficiary"  
- VA Form 21P-0847, "Request for Substitution of Claimant Upon Death Claimant" (can be submitted online)³

For filing a claim related to CHAMPVA, you may file online at the VA website, but no specific form is mentioned in the context²⁴.

If you are filing a claim for accelerated benefits, you need:

- Claim for Accelerated Benefits (SGLV 8284)⁷

**Summary:**

- For disability claims: VA Form 21-526EZ¹  
- For dependency and indemnity or accrued benefits: VA Forms 21P-534EZ, 21P-535, 21P-601, or 21P-0847³  
- For accelerated benefits: SGLV 8284⁷

### Question 4: CLAIMS-4

**Query:** What is a C&P exam?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 7.31s

**Answer:**

A C&P (Compensation and Pension) exam is a medical examination scheduled by the VA as part of the disability claims process. It is used to evaluate the severity of a veteran's service-connected disabilities to determine the appropriate disability rating and benefits. Veterans can request a specific contractor or VA medical center for their exam or ask for a tele-health appointment for certain types of exams, such as mental health. The VA will attempt to honor these requests if reasonable and possible. Veterans are usually scheduled for a C&P exam shortly after filing their claim, but in some cases, the exam may be delayed or not scheduled at all. It is important for veterans to keep their contact information up-to-date to avoid missing exam notifications¹⁵.

Veterans can also review Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs) related to their condition to understand how the C&P exam will be conducted. DBQs are forms used by examiners to document the severity of a disability and can sometimes be completed by a veteran's own physician to support a claim²³⁴⁶⁷.

**Sources:**
¹ [Compensation & Pension Examinations (C&P)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

² [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
³ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁴ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁶ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁷ [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 5: CLAIMS-5

**Query:** How do I prepare for a C&P exam?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 7.19s

**Answer:**

To prepare for a Compensation and Pension (C&P) exam, consider the following steps based on the provided information:

- Review the applicable Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQ) related to your condition. DBQs give you an idea of what the C&P exam will cover and the type of information the examiner will be looking for. You can also ask your treating physician to complete a DBQ on your behalf to submit with your claim. Examples of DBQs include those for cervical spine, thoracolumbar spine, traumatic brain injury (TBI), cranial nerve conditions, arthritis, fibromyalgia, and osteomyelitis³⁵⁷.

- If you have preferences regarding the location or type of your exam (for example, if you want a specific contractor, VA medical center, or a tele-health appointment), you can include a personal statement with your request. The VA will attempt to honor reasonable requests¹.

- Keep your contact information (mailing address, email, and phone number) up to date with the VA to ensure you receive timely notice of your exam appointment⁴.

- Attend the exam as scheduled. Missing a C&P exam, especially for reevaluations, can lead to loss of benefits or reduction in your disability rating⁶.

By reviewing the relevant DBQ and ensuring your contact information is current, you will be better prepared for your C&P exam.

### Question 6: CLAIMS-6

**Query:** What happens after I file a claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 3.11s

**Answer:**

After you file a claim, the next step involving the Veteran is typically the Compensation & Pension Exam (C&P). Veterans can expect to be scheduled for a C&P exam shortly after filing their claim. However, in some cases, a Veteran might not get an exam at all or there could be a delay of several months before one is scheduled. It is important to keep your mailing address, email address, and phone number up-to-date with the VA to avoid missing any notices about the examination¹.

### Question 7: CLAIMS-7

**Query:** Can I track my claim status?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 3.91s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can track your claim status. For appeals, you can check the status on VA.gov, by calling 1 (800) 827-1000, or by making a VERA appointment. For supplemental claims, the claim tracker will show statuses such as "We don't know your status" or "A reviewer is examining your new evidence" until the claim is closed out. Additionally, if you filed a Higher-Level Review (HLR) or Supplemental claim via QuickSubmit, it may take a week or two before it shows up online³.

### Question 8: CLAIMS-8

**Query:** What is an Intent to File?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.95s

**Answer:**

An Intent to File is the first step in filing a VA disability claim. It is important because it preserves your effective date, which determines the date from which you will receive payments. For recently separated Veterans, an Intent to File can be automatically opened the day after separation, and if filed before the one-year separation anniversary, it can extend the time to file a claim by an additional year while still allowing back pay from the day after separation¹.

An Intent to File can be submitted in several ways, including online at VA.gov, through a Veterans Service Officer (VSO), by phone, in person using VA Form 21-0966, by fax, or by mail. The VA uses the date the Intent to File is processed, not the postmark date. An Intent to File is valid for one year and can be used for one claim, which may cover multiple conditions. If multiple claims or appeals are filed on the same day, they will all fall under the same Intent to File².

**Sources:**
¹ [Intent to File - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Starting an Intent to File - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Intent to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Starting an Intent to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Confidence:** 0.6851351261138916

### Question 9: CLAIMS-9

**Query:** How do I get my service records?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 9.25s

**Answer:**

To get your service records, including awards, DD-214, enlistment/reenlistment paperwork, orders, and personnel actions, the method depends on your branch of service and the period when you separated:

- For Air Force, Army, Marine Corps, Navy, Coast Guard, Public Health Services, Space Force, and National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration, the process varies by separation date.
- If your branch and separation date fall under the category marked with an asterisk (*), you need to submit a request to the National Personnel Records Center (NPRC) via their veteran request portal.
- If your branch and separation date fall under the category marked with an at symbol (@), you can request your personnel file through the Defense Personnel Records Information System (DPRIS) by logging in at the specified website, then navigating to Correspondence/Documentation > Defense Personnel Records Information > Request Personnel File.

Here is a summary table example for some branches:

| Branch of Service | Period of Service Ended | How to Obtain |
|-------------------|------------------------|--------------|
| Air Force         | Before 10/1/2004       | Submit request to NPRC* |
| Air Force         | After 9/30/2004        | Use DPRIS@ |
| Army              | Before 10/1/1994       | Submit request to NPRC* |
| Army              | After 9/30/1994        | Use DPRIS@ |
| Marine Corps      | Before 1/1/1999        | Submit request to NPRC* |
| Marine Corps      | After 12/31/1998       | Use DPRIS@ |
| Navy              | Before 1/1/1995        | Submit request to NPRC* |
| Navy              | After 12/31/1994       | Use DPRIS@ |
| Coast Guard       | Any                    | Submit request to NPRC* |
| Space Force       | Any                    | Use DPRIS@ |

*Submit a request to NPRC at https://vetrecs.archives.gov/s/veteran-request  
@Access DPRIS at https://milconnect.dmdc.osd.mil/milconnect/protected/portlet/dpris and follow the path: Correspondence/Documentation > Defense Personnel Records Information > Request Personnel File.

This will allow you to obtain your military personnel records relevant to your service¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Military Personnel Records - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Military Personnel Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)
- [Service Treatment Records (STRs)/Military Medical Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)
- [2.Development for Medical Evidence, Service Records, and Stressor Information (Part 2)]()
- [2.Development for Medical Evidence, Service Records, and Stressor Information (Part 1)]()
- [Viewing Exam Reports](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Obtaining Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#obtainingrecords)

### Question 10: CLAIMS-10

**Query:** What is a buddy statement?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.53s

**Answer:**

A buddy statement, also known as a buddy letter, is a written statement from someone who can attest to your military service, changes in your behavior, or the severity of your symptoms when your military records are missing evidence or context. It is used as a piece of evidence to support your VA disability claim. This statement can be provided by someone who served with you, your friends and family, civilian coworkers or supervisors, or others such as a parole officer or priest.

**Sources:**

### Question 11: CLAIMS-11

**Query:** Do I need a lawyer to file a claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.83s

**Answer:**

You do not necessarily need a lawyer to file a claim. Hiring an attorney or claims agent is a personal decision. Unless your appeal or claim depends on a specific legal issue or angle, the benefit of having a lawyer may not be significant¹.

However, for certain types of claims, such as federal tort claims, it is highly recommended to seek an attorney who specializes in that area because these claims require you to do all the leg work and provide evidence yourself, as the federal government has no duty to assist in these cases⁵⁷.

For general VA disability claims, you can file on your own online, by mail, fax, or in person, or you can get assistance from a Veterans Service Officer (VSO) without needing a lawyer³⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)

⁷ [Winning a Federal Tort Claim - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#winningafederaltortc)

### Question 12: CLAIMS-12

**Query:** What is a nexus letter?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 3.56s

**Answer:**

A nexus letter is a written statement from a medically licensed and board-certified doctor that supports a veteran's disability claim by linking (or "nexusing") the veteran's current medical condition to their military service. The letter typically includes the doctor's credentials, the veteran's diagnosis, a review of the veteran's medical history and service records, and an explanation of how the veteran's condition is connected to their military service or why other risk factors are less likely causes. It serves as an independent medical opinion to help establish service connection for VA disability benefits.

**Sources:**
¹ [Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) / Nexus Letter - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#faq)  
² [Nexus Letter Example](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#nexusletterexample)

**Sources Cited:**

- [Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) / Nexus Letter - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#references)

### Question 13: CLAIMS-13

**Query:** How do I get a nexus letter?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 3.92s

**Answer:**

To get a nexus letter, you should ask a medically licensed and board-certified doctor who is familiar with your medical history and military service to write a statement supporting your disability claim. The letter should include:

- The doctor's credentials and specialty.
- Confirmation that you are their patient and the date since when.
- Your diagnosis with supporting medical evidence (e.g., lab work, X-rays).
- A review of your medical history, including service treatment records and other relevant medical records.
- A statement linking your current condition to events or circumstances during your military service.
- An explanation about the absence or presence of other risk factors that could have caused your condition.

While the letter does not need to be on official letterhead, having it on the doctor's practice letterhead is recommended to increase its authority and authenticity.

**Sources:**
¹ [Nexus Letter Example](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#nexusletterexample)  
² [Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) / Nexus Letter - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/imo#faq)

### Question 14: CLAIMS-14

**Query:** What evidence do I need for my claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 7.41s

**Answer:**

For your VA disability claim, you need to submit evidence that is both **new** and **relevant**:

- **New evidence** means the VA did not previously have or consider it.
- **Relevant evidence** directly addresses the reason your claim was denied.

Examples of new and relevant evidence include:

- Medical records showing you have been seen by a doctor for the claimed condition.
- A new theory of service connection, such as claiming your condition is caused by another service-connected disability.
- Evidence related to presumptive conditions.
- Willingness to attend a new VA examination if you missed a previous one.
- Independent medical opinions or nexus letters.
- Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs).
- Personal statements explaining your condition.
- Buddy statements from family, friends, or service members who can attest to your condition and its effects.

Additional types of evidence you may submit:

- Private medical records or non-VA hospital reports related to your condition or showing worsening symptoms (you must provide VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a if you want VA to obtain these).
- Newspaper articles or other documentation showing your involvement in relevant events.
- Supporting or personal statements to add context or highlight important details in your records.

For PTSD claims, a specific form (VA Form 21-0781) is generally required as supporting evidence.

Remember, you have **1 year** from the decision on your claim to file an appeal and submit additional evidence to support your claim¹³⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [2. Evidence Submission - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
³ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995) - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
⁴ [Miscellaneous Evidence - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 15: CLAIMS-15

**Query:** Can I file a claim while still on active duty?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 3.83s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can file a VA disability claim while still on active duty. If you have 90-180 days before separation, you may file a Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) claim, which ideally allows you to get a claims decision the day after you separate. Even if you have less than 90 days before separation, you can and should still file a claim. It is also important to obtain a copy of your military medical records before separating and to document every issue you have had on your medical forms to support your claim.

### Question 16: CLAIMS-16

**Query:** What is BDD (Benefits Delivery at Discharge)?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.65s

**Answer:**

Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) is a VA program that allows active duty servicemembers who are between 180 and 90 days before separation to file a disability claim within that window. The goal of BDD is to enable the claim to be processed so that the veteran can receive a decision on their claim the day after they separate from active duty. However, this outcome is not guaranteed. To participate in BDD, servicemembers must submit their Service Treatment Records (STRs) no later than 90 days before separation, including the Separation Health Assessment - Part A Self-Assessment (SHA, Part A). Private medical records are generally not included in STRs unless the veteran arranges to have them added. Veterans do not need to complete all required exams before separation or before the filing window closes. It is important to keep the VA updated with current contact information to receive notifications about examinations and claim status¹⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/digsystem)  
⁴ [Active Duty Servicemembers - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 17: CLAIMS-17

**Query:** How do I file for an increase?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 3.38s

**Answer:**

To file for an increase in your VA disability rating:

- Review the VA Rating Schedule to determine if your current disability level qualifies for an increase.
- File your increase claim on the va.gov website.
- The VA will order new exams to assess the current severity of your condition(s).
- You do not need to supply evidence that your condition has worsened.
- Be aware that filing for an increase opens your entire claims file for review, which can potentially help or hurt your overall claim, though most raters focus only on the conditions you are requesting an increase for unless there is an obvious error¹.

**Sources:**

### Question 18: CLAIMS-18

**Query:** What is a supplemental claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.78s

**Answer:**

A supplemental claim is an opportunity to submit new and relevant evidence to the VA to support your claim. "New" means the VA did not previously have or consider the evidence, and "relevant" means the evidence directly addresses the reason your claim was denied. For example, if your claim was denied due to lack of diagnosis, submitting evidence of a diagnosis would be relevant.

New and relevant evidence can include:

- Being seen by your doctor for the claimed issue(s);
- A new theory of service connection (such as claiming your condition was caused by another service-connected disability);
- Evidence related to presumptive conditions;
- Willingness to attend new VA examinations if you were previously denied for missing one;
- Independent medical opinions or nexus letters;
- Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs);
- Personal or buddy statements.

You have one year from the decision on your claim to file a supplemental claim to potentially keep your effective date and back pay. It is recommended to wait until you have sufficient evidence before filing. Supplemental claims are reviewed by a VA rater, and it is typical to receive new exams or medical opinions when you file one, provided the appeal properly addresses the denial rationale.

**Sources:**
¹ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
² [Supplemental Claim - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#supplementalclaim)  
⁶ [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)

### Question 20: CLAIMS-20

**Query:** Can I reopen a denied claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.46s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can reopen a denied claim by submitting new and relevant evidence that was not previously considered by the VA. This new evidence must directly address the reasons for the prior denial. Examples of new and relevant evidence include:

- New medical records or diagnosis related to the claimed condition.
- A new theory of service connection, such as claiming the condition was caused by another service-connected disability.
- Independent medical opinions or nexus letters.
- Personal or buddy statements.
- Willingness to attend a VA examination if you missed one previously.

You have one year from the date of the decision to file an appeal or submit additional evidence to potentially keep your back pay. Filing a supplemental claim using VA Form 20-0995 is the typical method to reopen a claim with new and relevant evidence¹⁶. 

If you believe the VA made an error in evaluating your claim, you might also consider filing a Higher-Level Review (HLR)¹⁷.

### Question 22: CLAIMS-22

**Query:** What is aggravation?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 8.36s

**Answer:**

Aggravation refers to the worsening of a condition due to military service or a service-connected condition. There are two types of aggravation:

1. Aggravation of a preexisting (pre-service) condition by military service.
2. Aggravation of an existing (post-service) condition by a service-connected condition.

In both cases, the condition becomes service-connected because the military service or a service-connected condition has worsened it beyond its natural progression¹.

For aggravation of an existing (post-service) condition, the VA must establish the level of severity before and after aggravation and obtain a medical opinion that the service-connected condition worsened the existing condition at least as likely as not².

For aggravation of a preexisting (pre-service) condition, the VA uses evidence such as the Military Entrance Processing Station (MEPS) exam and medical records to determine if the condition existed before service and was worsened by service⁴.

Rating of aggravated conditions involves subtracting the preexisting condition's severity from the current severity to determine the aggravated rating, with some special rules depending on whether the condition was pre-service or post-service⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)  
² [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)  
⁴ [Aggravation of a Preexisting (Pre-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofapreexistingpreservicecondition)  
⁵ [Rating Aggravated Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#ratingaggravatedconditions)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)
- [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#references)
- [Aggravation of a Preexisting (Pre-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofapreexistingpreservicecondition)
- [Rating Aggravated Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#ratingaggravatedconditions)

### Question 23: CLAIMS-23

**Query:** How do I prove service connection?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.18s

**Answer:**

To prove service connection for a disability claim, you need to establish the following:

- A current diagnosis of the disability.
- An in-service event, injury, or disease that caused or contributed to the disability.
- A medical nexus showing that it is at least as likely as not that the disability is related to your military service or a service-connected condition.

If any of these elements are missing, your claim may be denied. To strengthen your claim, you can submit additional evidence such as:

- An Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) or Nexus letter.
- Personal statements describing your condition and its connection to service.
- Buddy statements from others who can support your claim.

If you were told you missed a VA exam, you should file a supplemental claim and state that you are willing and able to attend exams at this time.

If you believe the VA did not properly evaluate your evidence, you may consider filing a Higher-Level Review (HLR).

**Sources:**
¹ [Denied Service-Connection - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#deniedserviceconnection)  
² [Denied Service-Connection - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Denied Service-Connection](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#deniedserviceconnection)
- [Denied Service-Connection](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)

### Question 25: CLAIMS-25

**Query:** Can I file a claim years after discharge?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.37s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can file a claim years after discharge. There is no specific restriction mentioned in the provided context about filing a claim long after separation from service. However, it is important to note that if you file a new claim while another claim is active, the VA will close the newly filed claim automatically but will eventually combine the newly claimed issues with the older active claim¹.

Additionally, if you are filing an appeal or acting on a pending disability claim related to a deceased Veteran, there are time limits such as acting within one year of the Veteran's death or the claim's decision, but these do not restrict filing a new claim years after discharge generally⁶.

For best results, it is recommended to have medical evidence and documentation ready before filing, and to file as soon as possible to avoid losing potential back pay due to processing delays¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁶ [Time Limits](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 26: APPEAL-1

**Query:** How do I appeal a VA decision?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 7.04s

**Answer:**

To appeal a VA decision, you have three initial methods to choose from:

- Higher-Level Review
- Supplemental Claim
- Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA)

You may use the assistance of a Veterans Service Organization (VSO), lawyer, or an accredited claims agent to help with your appeal. It is important to file your appeal within one year of your claim's decision date if you want to keep your effective date (back pay). Filing an appeal invites the VA to review your file, and if a Clear and Unmistakable Error (CUE) is found or your condition has improved, the VA may adjust your rating accordingly.

If you choose to appeal to the Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA), you can submit your appeal online, via your VSO or lawyer, by fax, or by mail. The contact details for submitting by fax or mail are:

- Fax: 844-678-8979
- Mail: Board of Veterans' Appeals, P.O. Box 27063, Washington, DC 20038

If your appeal is denied again, you may try another eligible option. If you disagree with a BVA decision and have new and relevant evidence, you can file a Supplemental Claim or appeal to the U.S. Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims.

Remember, you cannot file multiple appeals for the same issue simultaneously without withdrawing the current appeal first.

For checking the status of your appeal, you can visit VA.gov, call 1 (800) 827-1000, or make a VERA appointment.

**Sources:**

¹ [Filing an Appeal](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#filinganappeal)  
² [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)  
³ [Submitting to BVA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)
- [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)

### Question 27: APPEAL-2

**Query:** What is a Higher Level Review?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 8.01s

**Answer:**

A Higher-Level Review (HLR) is an entirely new review of your VA claim conducted by a more experienced, senior rater known as a Decision Review Officer (DRO). This review involves re-examining the existing evidence of record without considering any new evidence submitted after the original decision was made. The DRO has the authority to issue a "difference of opinion," meaning they can weigh the evidence differently than the previous rater, potentially resulting in a favorable outcome for the veteran. However, this difference of opinion cannot lead to a negative outcome for the veteran. If there were any Clear and Unmistakable Errors (CUEs) in the prior decision, these can be corrected even if the correction results in a negative outcome, provided proper due notice is given.

Key points about Higher-Level Review include:

- New evidence received after the decision cannot be considered.
- Intents to file do not apply to HLRs.
- Veterans cannot file back-to-back HLRs but can alternate between HLRs and supplemental claims.
- Veterans cannot request an HLR after a Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA) denial.
- The HLR must be submitted within one year of the claim's decision.

If the senior rater identifies any Duty to Assist (DTA) errors during the HLR, they will issue a rating decision and close the HLR, noting the errors. These errors will be addressed in a subsequent supplemental claim, which allows new and relevant evidence to be submitted and is decided by non-DRO raters.

Overall, the HLR is a faster, evidence-based review process focused on re-evaluating the existing record by a more experienced adjudicator without introducing new evidence or starting a new claim process³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Higher-Level Review (HLR 20-0996)](https://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0996-ARE.pdf)  
² [Higher-Level Review (Part 2) - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
³ [Higher-Level Review (Part 1) - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Higher-Level Review [(HLR 20-0996)](https://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0996-ARE.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Higher-Level Review (Part 2)]()
- [Higher-Level Review (Part 1)]()
- [1\. Direct Review](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [Ratersâ€™ Discretion (Rating Wiggle)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)
- [Low Rating](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#lowrating)
- [Low Rating](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 28: APPEAL-3

**Query:** What is a Supplemental Claim?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 4.61s

**Answer:**

A Supplemental Claim is an opportunity to submit **new and relevant** evidence to the VA to support a previously denied claim. "New" means the VA did not previously have or consider the evidence, and "relevant" means the evidence directly addresses the reason for the denial. For example, if a claim was denied due to lack of diagnosis, submitting evidence of a diagnosis would be relevant.

New and relevant evidence can include:

- Being seen by a doctor for the claimed issue(s)
- A new theory of service connection (such as claiming the condition was caused by another service-connected disability)
- Evidence related to presumptive conditions
- Willingness to attend new VA examinations if previously missed
- Independent medical opinions or nexus letters
- Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs)
- Personal statements or buddy statements

You have one year from the decision on your claim to file a Supplemental Claim to potentially preserve your effective date and back pay. It is generally recommended to wait until you have sufficient evidence before filing.

**Sources:**
¹ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
² [Supplemental Claim - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#supplementalclaim)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Supplemental Claim [(20-0995)](http://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0995-ARE.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)
- [Supplemental Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#supplementalclaim)
- [Submitting a Supplemental Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)

- [38 USC 1151 Claims (VA Negligence) - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#faq)

### Question 29: APPEAL-4

**Query:** What is the Board of Veterans Appeals?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 5.99s

**Answer:**

The Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA) is a body that reviews appeals by veterans regarding their VA claims. The review is conducted by Veterans Law Judges who are not bound by VA Central Office policy decisions and have the authority to issue grants that VA raters cannot. This means BVA decisions can sometimes contradict VA policies. Veterans must submit their appeal to the BVA within one year of their claim's decision, and they cannot request a BVA appeal after receiving a BVA decision for the same claimed item consecutively. The BVA offers three appeal lanes for veterans to choose from: Direct Review, Evidence Submission, and Hearing. The process can take years, so it is usually chosen after other appeal options like Supplemental Claim or Higher Level Review have been exhausted.

**Sources:**
¹ [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
² [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#boardveteransappealsbvaakanoticeofdisagreementnodaka)

### Question 31: APPEAL-6

**Query:** What is a Notice of Disagreement?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.49s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.9%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

A Notice of Disagreement (NOA) is a document submitted by an appellant to express disagreement with a Board decision. It is one of the examples of documents that can be used to disagree with Board decisions, as permitted by law. The NOA is submitted pursuant to 38 U.S.C. 7266¹.

### Question 32: APPEAL-7

**Query:** Can I get a hearing for my appeal?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.08s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get a hearing for your appeal. The hearing can be conducted by videoconference or in-person in Washington, DC. After your hearing, you will have 90 days to submit any new evidence. The judge will then make a decision considering both the hearing and the evidence of record¹.

### Question 33: APPEAL-8

**Query:** What is a Board hearing?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.31s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.9%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

A Board hearing is an opportunity for a veteran to present their case before a Veterans Law Judge either by videoconference or in-person in Washington, DC. After the hearing, the veteran has 90 days to submit any new evidence. The judge will then make a decision based on the hearing and the evidence of record¹. This hearing is one of the appeal lanes available when submitting an appeal to the Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA)².

### Question 34: APPEAL-9

**Query:** Should I get a lawyer for my appeal?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.88s

**Answer:**

Whether you should get a lawyer for your appeal is a personal decision. According to the information provided, unless your appeal depends on a specific legal issue or angle, the benefit of hiring an attorney or claims agent may not be significant. Additionally, lawyers and claims agents cannot charge more than 33.3% of your back pay and only get paid if you win your appeal. Veterans Service Organizations (VSOs) can assist you for free, which might be a valuable resource to consider before hiring a lawyer¹³.

### Question 35: APPEAL-10

**Query:** What happens if I miss the appeal deadline?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.22s

**Answer:**

If you miss the appeal deadline to file a notice of appeal (NOA) with the Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims (CAVC), you may still be able to file if you can show "good cause" as to why the NOA could not be filed timely. Alternatively, you can file a Motion for Reconsideration (MFR) with the Board. However, it is ultimately up to the CAVC to decide if the NOA was timely filed or if good cause exists to accept a late filing¹.

### Question 37: APPEAL-12

**Query:** What is a remand?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.9s

**Answer:**

A remand is a case that has been sent back by the Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA) or the Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims (CAVC) to the VA regional office or Decision Review Officer (DRO) for further development, additional evidence gathering, or other actions before a final decision can be made. Remands are among the oldest cases and must be worked on a priority basis, with special attention and expeditious processing mandated by law.

**Sources:**
¹ [Requirements for Expeditious Processing of Remands (7.G.3.c) - (appeals)](Not available)

### Question 38: APPEAL-13

**Query:** Can I submit new evidence on appeal?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.87s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can submit new evidence on appeal. Specifically:

- If your appeal has become a supplemental claim (for example, after a duty to assist error was found), you are free to submit any new and relevant evidence to your claim¹.
- When submitting a Notice of Disagreement (NOD), you can submit new evidence within 90 days from the day your NOD is received³.
- A supplemental claim allows you to submit new and relevant evidence that was not previously considered or available, such as new medical opinions, personal statements, or new diagnoses⁶.

Therefore, submitting new evidence is allowed and encouraged to support your appeal.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)  
³ [2. Evidence Submission](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)

### Question 39: APPEAL-14

**Query:** What is a Statement of the Case?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.39s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.9%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

A Statement of the Case (SOC) is a document related to the Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA) process. It is a factor in determining the time-frames for reopening a BVA appeal. Specifically, if you have received a Statement of the Case, you have 12 months from the date of the SOC to submit a VA Form 9. If you have not received an SOC, you have 12 months from the decision you were appealing to submit a VA Form 10182¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Statement of the Case (SOC) - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#soc#statementofthecasesoc)

### Question 40: APPEAL-15

**Query:** How do I choose an appeal lane?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 7.3s

**Answer:**

The context does not provide specific information on how to choose an appeal lane. However, it does mention that when filing an appeal, you can submit one Board Appeal form and attach additional pages to explain how you want your issues reviewed, or submit multiple Board Appeal forms for different review options¹. Also, if you want to change your review option at the Board, you must fill out a new Board Appeal form, check the box for the new review option, and list all issues, with a deadline of one year from the decision date or 60 days from your Board Appeal submission, whichever is later⁶.

For more detailed guidance on choosing an appeal lane, it is recommended to carefully read your Decision Letter, which should outline what you need to do for a successful appeal⁴ ⁷.

### Question 41: APPEAL-16

**Query:** What is the Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.28s

**Answer:**

The Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims (CAVC) is a court that reviews decisions made by the Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA). The judges at the CAVC have broad authority to interpret the laws and regulations that govern the Veterans Benefits Administration (VBA). Their decisions create case law that can change how the VBA operates. Veterans have 120 days after their BVA decision to file an appeal directly with the CAVC, not through the VA. While veterans can represent themselves, it is strongly recommended to seek legal representation at this stage¹³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims (CAVC) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
³ [Higher Courts (Courts above BVA) - (appeals)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#highercourtscourtsabovebva)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims (CAVC)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)
- [[Court of Appeals for the Federal Circuit (CAFC)](http://www.cafc.uscourts.gov/)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)
- [Higher Courts (Courts above BVA)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#highercourtscourtsabovebva)
- [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA [10182](https://www.va.gov/vaforms/va/pdf/VA10182.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)
- [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)

### Question 42: APPEAL-17

**Query:** Can I appeal a C&P exam?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 6.09s

**Answer:**

The context does not explicitly state whether you can directly appeal a C&P (Compensation and Pension) exam itself. However, it does provide information related to appeals of rating decisions and claims that involve C&P exams:

- If you file an appeal, the VA may order new exams or medical opinions, especially if you file a supplemental claim¹.
- If you believe there was an error in the rating decision related to your claim, you can file a Higher-Level Review (HLR) or appeal to the Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA)².
- Appeals can lead to the VA reviewing your file and potentially reducing or increasing your rating based on new evidence or exams³.

In summary, while you cannot directly appeal a C&P exam, you can appeal the rating decision that results from the exam. During the appeal process, you may be scheduled for a new C&P exam or medical opinion.

### Question 43: APPEAL-18

**Query:** What if my appeal is denied?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.59s

**Answer:**

If your appeal is denied, you have a couple of options:

- If you disagree with the Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA) decision and have new and relevant evidence, you can file a Supplemental Claim to try to support your case further.
- Alternatively, you can appeal to the U.S. Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims.

These options allow you to continue pursuing your claim despite the denial¹.

### Question 45: APPEAL-20

**Query:** What is duty to assist error?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.18s

**Answer:**

A duty to assist error occurs when the VA fails to fulfill its obligation to help a Veteran obtain the evidence necessary to support their claim or appeal. One common example of a duty to assist error is the failure to send the foundational notice known as the "5103 notice," which explains what evidence is needed to properly support the claim or appeal¹.

### Question 46: RATING-1

**Query:** How are VA disability ratings calculated?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.63s

**Answer:**

VA disability ratings are calculated based on the severity of a Veteran's service-connected disabilities using the Schedule for Rating Disabilities. The process involves:

- Assigning a percentage rating to each service-connected condition based on the severity of symptoms and evidence, as detailed in the rating schedule¹.
- Considering all submitted evidence such as Compensation & Pension (C&P) exam reports, Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs), nexus letters, buddy statements, personal statements, and treatment notes to determine the totality of symptoms for each condition¹.
- Using diagnostic codes to identify specific conditions and their corresponding rating criteria¹.
- When multiple disabilities are present, combining the individual ratings using the VA's combined ratings table and applying the bilateral factor if applicable².

The final combined rating reflects the overall level of disability and determines the Veteran's compensation and eligibility for related benefits.

**Sources:**
¹ [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)  
² [VA Math - Combined Ratings Calculation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#references)

**Sources Cited:**
- [External VA Resources and Calculators](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/resources#externalvaresourcesandcalculators)
- [VA Math - Combined Ratings Calculation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#references)
- [Common VA Diagnostic Codes Reference](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [70%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/combinedbenefits)
- [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [How a Rater Decides](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)
- [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)

### Question 47: RATING-2

**Query:** What is combined rating?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.14s

**Answer:**

A combined rating is the overall disability rating assigned by the VA when a veteran has multiple service-connected conditions. Instead of simply adding the individual ratings together, the VA uses a specific combined ratings table to calculate the total combined rating. This method accounts for the fact that a veteran cannot be more than 100% disabled and that the impact of multiple disabilities is not purely additive.

For example, if a veteran has a 30% rating for one condition and a 10% rating for another, the combined rating is not 40%, but rather 37% according to the combined ratings table. Additionally, if the veteran has bilateral disabilities (affecting both sides of the body), a bilateral factor of 10% is applied to the combined rating of those bilateral conditions, increasing the overall combined rating further. The final combined rating is rounded to the nearest whole number and cannot exceed 100%¹ ⁶ ⁷.

**Sources:**
¹ [VA Math - Combined Ratings Calculation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#references)  
⁶ [Bilateral Factor (Part 2) - (rating_calculation)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#combined)  
⁷ [Bilateral Factor (Part 2) - (rating_calculation)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#combined)

**Sources Cited:**
- [VA Math - Combined Ratings Calculation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#references)
- [VA Math - Combined Ratings Calculation - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#faq)
- [Federal Benefits List by Combined Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/combinedbenefits)
- [Single Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin)
- [Multiple Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin)
- [Bilateral Factor (Part 2)]()
- [Bilateral Factor (Part 2)]()

### Question 48: RATING-3

**Query:** How does VA math work?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.16s

**Answer:**

VA math, or the VA combined ratings calculation, is the method used by the Department of Veterans Affairs to combine multiple disability ratings into a single overall disability rating. This process is not a simple addition of percentages but uses a specific combined ratings table as outlined in 38 CFR § 4.25. The combined rating reflects the overall level of disability and determines compensation.

Key points about VA math include:

- The combined rating is calculated using a combined ratings table, not by adding percentages directly. This ensures the total rating does not exceed 100% inappropriately.  
- If a veteran has disabilities affecting both sides of the body (bilateral disabilities), a bilateral factor may be applied to increase the combined rating slightly. The application of the bilateral factor is governed by 38 CFR § 4.26 and specific VA guidelines.  
- Veterans can check if their conditions have been given the bilateral factor by reviewing their VA codesheet.  
- The maximum combined rating is 100%, but under certain circumstances, veterans may receive Special Monthly Compensation (SMC), which can result in payments higher than the 100% rate.  

For detailed legal references and guidance, the combined ratings table and bilateral factor rules are found in the Code of Federal Regulations and VA manuals cited in the VA Math references¹.  

**Sources:**  
¹ [VA Math - Combined Ratings Calculation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#references)  
² [VA Math - Combined Ratings Calculation - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#faq)  
⁴ [VA Math - Combined Ratings Calculation - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#faq)

**Sources Cited:**
- [VA Math - Combined Ratings Calculation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#references)

### Question 49: RATING-4

**Query:** What is the bilateral factor?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.11s

**Answer:**

The bilateral factor is an additional 10% added to the combined VA disability rating when a Veteran has a rated condition (more than 0%) that affects both the left and right sides of the same extremity half—either the upper extremities (above the waist) or the lower extremities (below the waist). The bilateral conditions are first combined using VA math, and then an additional 10% of that combined rating is added. This adjusted rating is then combined with the rest of the disabilities to calculate the final combined rating³⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Bilateral Factor (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#bilateral)  
² [Bilateral Factor (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#bilateral)  
³ [Bilateral Factor (Part 2)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#combined)  
⁴ [Bilateral Factor (Part 2)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vamath#combined)

### Question 50: RATING-5

**Query:** Can I get 100% disability?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 6.7s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get a 100% disability rating from the VA. If your combined service-connected disabilities total 100%, you may be rated at 100%. Additionally, if you are unable to maintain substantially gainful employment due to your service-connected disabilities, you may qualify for Total Disability based on Individual Unemployability (TDIU), which is considered a 100% rating for benefits purposes even if your combined rating is less than 100%¹.

You can also receive a temporary 100% rating during hospitalization for certain conditions if the medical evidence supports it, showing the condition causing hospitalization and the length of stay⁷.

If you have non-static conditions (conditions that may improve or worsen), you can still be considered permanently and totally disabled (P&T) if the conditions that make up your 100% rating or TDIU are static (not expected to improve)⁴. For example, active cancer is considered a non-static condition but does not affect the P&T status if the rest of your rating is static³⁴.

To check if you are already rated P&T, you can view your VA Benefit Summary letter on va.gov, which will state if you are considered totally and permanently disabled due to your service-connected disabilities⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
³ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
⁴ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
⁵ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
⁷ [Filing for Temporary 100%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/temp100)

### Question 51: RATING-6

**Query:** What is the difference between schedular and TDIU?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.36s

**Answer:**

The difference between schedular and TDIU (Total Disability Individual Unemployability) lies primarily in how the disability rating is determined and the Veteran's ability to work:

- **Schedular Rating**: This is the disability rating assigned based on the VA's rating schedule for specific disabilities. It reflects the average impairment in earning capacity caused by the service-connected condition(s) as outlined in the rating criteria.

- **TDIU (Total Disability Individual Unemployability)**: This is a special type of rating granted when a Veteran's service-connected disabilities, regardless of their combined schedular rating, prevent them from securing or maintaining substantially gainful employment. To qualify for TDIU, the Veteran must have at least one service-connected disability rated at 60% or higher and be unable to hold a steady job due to their disabilities. Marginal or odd jobs do not disqualify a Veteran from TDIU. TDIU essentially compensates the Veteran at the 100% disability rate based on unemployability rather than the schedular rating alone¹⁷.

In summary, schedular ratings are based on medical impairment percentages, while TDIU is based on the Veteran's inability to work due to service-connected disabilities, potentially granting a 100% rating even if the combined schedular rating is less than 100%¹ ⁷.

**Sources:**
¹ [Unemployability - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)  
⁷ [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Unemployability](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)
- [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)
- [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)
- [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)
- [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#permanentandtotalptdisability)
- [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)
- [50%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)

### Question 53: RATING-8

**Query:** What is SMC (Special Monthly Compensation)?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.82s

**Answer:**

Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) is an additional tax-free benefit that can be paid to veterans who have service-connected disabilities that result in the need for regular aid and attendance or other specific severe disabilities. For example, if a veteran's traumatic brain injury (TBI) causes them to require regular aid and attendance, they may qualify for SMC¹. 

SMC is generally not paid in addition to the veteran's regular disability compensation rate, with some exceptions such as SMC-K and the obsolete SMC-Q².

**Sources:**
¹ [Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/TBI)

### Question 55: RATING-10

**Query:** What is housebound status?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.23s

**Answer:**

Housebound status refers to a condition where a veteran is substantially confined to their dwelling and immediate premises due to service-connected disabilities. This confinement must be permanent or expected to continue throughout the veteran's lifetime. The veteran may leave occasionally for medical appointments or other necessary trips, but these do not disqualify housebound status. Typically, the veteran should have service-connected disabilities that combine to at least 100% to meet this requirement. If institutionalized, the confinement applies to the ward or clinical areas of the institution.

There is also a statutory housebound status, which applies when a veteran has a single 100% disability rating plus a separate single 60% rating, or multiple ratings that combine to at least 60%. However, a single disease process that combines separate evaluations to 100% does not count as a singular 100% rating for this purpose².

Housebound status can result in eligibility for Special Monthly Compensation (SMC S) or increased VA Pension benefits, depending on whether the condition is service-connected or non-service-connected.

**Sources:**
¹ [SMC S (Housebound)/(Statutory Housebound)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/smc#smcshouseboundstatutoryhousebound)  
² [Housebound - Special Monthly Pension - (pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-housebound#houseboundspecialmonthlypension)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Housebound - Special Monthly Pension](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-housebound#houseboundspecialmonthlypension)

### Question 56: RATING-11

**Query:** What is aid and attendance?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 6.47s

**Answer:**

Aid and Attendance is a benefit that provides additional financial assistance to veterans who require help with daily living activities due to disabilities. It can be awarded as part of an increased VA Pension for veterans with non-service connected conditions, or as Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) for those with service-connected disabilities.

Specifically, Aid and Attendance under SMC L is awarded to veterans who meet one or more of the following criteria:

- Anatomical loss or loss of use of both feet;
- Anatomical loss or loss of use of one hand and one foot;
- Loss of sight or blindness in both eyes;
- Permanently bedridden; or
- Need daily help with basic needs such as eating, dressing, and bathing.

Additionally, a veteran may qualify if their disability (mental or physical) requires another person to regularly help prevent them from harming themselves or others.

An important policy note is that the service-connected disabilities causing the need for Aid and Attendance generally must be from a single disease process that combines to a 100% rating or has an evaluation of 100%. If this is not met, an advisory opinion from VA Central Office is required, which can be difficult to obtain favorably. Veterans denied Aid and Attendance may appeal to the Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA)¹³.

Aid and Attendance is distinct from non-service connected Aid and Attendance, which is part of an increased VA Pension benefit, whereas service-connected Aid and Attendance is part of Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) benefits¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Aid and Attendance (Pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#aidandattendancepension)  
³ [SMC L (Aid and Attendance)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/smc#smclaidandattendance)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Aid and Attendance (Pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#aidandattendancepension)

### Question 57: RATING-12

**Query:** Can my rating be reduced?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.81s

**Answer:**

Yes, your VA disability rating can be reduced under certain circumstances:

- By filing an appeal, the VA will review your file and if they find a Clear and Unmistakable Error (CUE) where you were rated too high or service connection was granted in error, they will take action to correct that, which can include reducing your rating¹.

- If exams ordered as part of your claim show that your condition has improved, the VA can start action to reduce your rating¹.

- However, if your rating has been held for at least 5 continuous years, a single exam showing improvement is generally not enough for a reduction. The VA must show continuous improvement, typically requiring more than one exam showing sustained improvement before proposing a reduction²³.

- There are exceptions, such as conditions rated based on lab results (e.g., kidney or lung function), where an improvement shown by a single lab test may lead to a reduction if you filed an increase claim and the new test confirms improvement²³.

- Note that this 5-year protection does not reset after 5 years; improvements shown later can still lead to reductions²³.

In summary, your rating can be reduced if there is a clear error or sustained improvement in your condition, but protections exist to prevent reductions based on a single exam after 5 years of a stable rating.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)  
² [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)  
³ [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Filing an Appeal - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#faq)
- [Low Rating](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 58: RATING-13

**Query:** What is a protected rating?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.39s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.3%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

A protected rating refers to a VA disability rating that cannot be reduced for a certain period of time after it has been assigned or increased. Specifically:

- When a veteran's rating is increased, the previous rating percentage may have protection for 5, 10, or 20 years.
- The 5-year protection applies only to the original rating percentage before the increase, not the newly increased rating.
- For example, if a veteran had a 10% rating for 10 years and then it was increased to 30%, the 10% rating retains its 5-year protection, but the new 30% rating does not have that 5-year protection initially.
- The 20-year protection applies only to the original rating that has been held for 20 years, not the increased rating.
- This means a veteran's rating is "protected" from reduction for a set period, depending on how long the rating has been held, but this protection applies differently to original and increased ratings.

**Sources:**
¹ [Example Involving Increase - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)  
² [Example Involving Increase - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

### Question 59: RATING-14

**Query:** What is static vs dynamic rating?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 6.07s

**Answer:**

A static rating refers to a service-connected disability rating that is not subject to regular future evaluations or reexaminations by the VA. These conditions are considered stable and unlikely to improve or worsen, so their rating remains fixed unless there is a clear error or new evidence. In contrast, dynamic ratings are those that can be reevaluated over time because the condition may improve or worsen, affecting the disability percentage.

Most conditions rated by the VA are now made static from the outset, meaning they do not require ongoing reevaluation. Veterans can check which of their conditions are static or dynamic online or by reviewing their C-File code sheet. Static conditions are important because they factor into whether a veteran can be granted permanent and total (P&T) disability status, which generally requires that the combined rating be based on static conditions.

In summary:

- **Static ratings:** Conditions not subject to regular future evaluations; rating remains fixed unless corrected for error or new evidence¹³.
- **Dynamic ratings:** Conditions that can be reevaluated over time due to potential changes in severity¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Static vs Dynamic Ratings - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/static-check#faq)  
³ [Static vs Dynamic Ratings - (rating_info)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/static-check#staticvsdynamicratings)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Static vs Dynamic Ratings - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/static-check#faq)
- [Static vs Dynamic Ratings - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/static-check#faq)
- [Static vs Dynamic Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/static-check#staticvsdynamicratings)

### Question 60: RATING-15

**Query:** How do I get backpay?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.93s

**Answer:**

To get back pay from the VA for disability benefits, here are some key points to understand:

- The effective date of your claim determines when your back pay starts. However, the month of your effective date is **not** paid out.
- VA pays benefits in arrears, meaning payments are made at the end of the month.
- If you believe your back pay amount is incorrect after accounting for these factors, you can call the VA at 1-800-827-1000 and request an audit to review your payments.
- Unexpected money you receive may be back pay resulting from a positive claim decision or from an audit that found you were underpaid.

So, to get back pay, you must have a claim with an established effective date, and if payments seem off, you can request a VA audit to correct it.

**Sources:**
¹ [VA Money Matters and Back Pay - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/money#faq)  
² [VA Money Matters and Back Pay - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/money#faq)

**Sources Cited:**

- [VA Money Matters and Back Pay - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/money#faq)

### Question 61: RATING-16

**Query:** What is an effective date?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.43s

**Answer:**

An effective date is the date from which a veteran's benefits are considered to begin. It determines when compensation or Dependency and Indemnity Compensation (DIC) payments start. For certain claims, such as 1151 grants, the effective date can be either:

- The date of the injury or aggravation if the claim is received within one year of the incident; or
- The date the claim was initiated.

For DIC, the effective date can be:

- The first day of the month in which the Veteran’s death occurred if a claim is received within one year following the date of death; or
- The date the claim was received.

**Sources:**
¹ [Effective Dates - (claims_special)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#effectivedates)

### Question 63: RATING-18

**Query:** What benefits come with 100% disability?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.92s

**Answer:**

Veterans with a 100% disability rating receive several significant benefits, including:

- Eligibility for Special Monthly Compensation (SMC), which can provide additional financial support beyond the basic 100% rating¹.
- Many additional benefits that other 100% Veterans are not entitled to, such as:
  - Education benefits including entitlement to Chapter 35 Dependents Educational Assistance (DEA) and some states offering free college to Veterans and their dependents.
  - Financial benefits like removal of the 10% penalty for early withdrawal of 401K and IRAs.
  - Health care benefits including eligibility for Veteran's dependents to enroll in CHAMPVA and the ability to freely use non-VA emergency rooms during a medical emergency with reimbursement claims.
  - Housing benefits such as property tax exemptions in some states.
  - Social Security benefits including expedited processing of Veteran’s disability claims.
  - Travel benefits like free air travel on Space-A (military flights) within the U.S. and certain territories¹.

These benefits are in addition to the standard benefits that come with a 100% rating, such as priority VA health care and financial compensation.

**Sources:**
¹ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#additionalbenefits)

### Question 64: RATING-19

**Query:** What is P&T (Permanent and Total)?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.2s

**Answer:**

Permanent and Total (P&T) disability is a special status for Veterans that means they are paid at the 100% disability rate (total) and their disabilities are considered static (permanent). However, "permanent" is somewhat of a misnomer because the VA can still reduce the evaluation unless the Veteran has held the rating for at least 20 years, except in cases of fraud. Veterans with P&T status do not have regularly scheduled reexaminations unless specific conditions apply. The decision letter does not explicitly state "Permanent and Total," but it will show entitlement to Dependents Educational Assistance (DEA) as an indicator of P&T status¹.

**Sources:**

### Question 65: RATING-20

**Query:** What is the 5 year rule?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.88s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **47.4%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

The 5 year rule refers to the protection a Veteran has against reduction of their disability rating based on a single Compensation & Pension (C&P) exam showing improvement. Specifically:

- A Veteran's rating generally cannot be reduced based on just one exam that shows improvement if the evaluation has been held for at least 5 continuous years.
- Continuous improvement must be shown, meaning it usually requires more than one exam showing the condition is still improved before the VA will issue a proposal for reduction.
- If the reduction would lower the combined evaluation, the Veteran will receive a proposal for reduction; otherwise, the condition may be reduced without notice.
- This protection does not reset after 5 years. For example, if an exam shows improvement at year 6 and another at year 16, the VA can still seek to reduce the rating based on those improvements.
- There are exceptions, such as conditions rated based on lab results (e.g., kidney or lung function), where a single improved lab result can lead to reduction, especially if the Veteran filed an increase claim for that condition.

**Sources:**
¹ [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)  
² [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 66: RATING-21

**Query:** What is the 10 year rule?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.82s

**Answer:**

The 10 year rule refers to the protection of a Veteran's service-connection for a disability after 10 years from the date of the original grant. Specifically:

- A Veteran's service-connection for a disability cannot be severed after 10 years, except in cases of fraud or if military records clearly show the Veteran did not have the required service or character of discharge.
- Erroneously service-connected conditions are still eligible for rating increases if warranted.
- Disabilities caused by an erroneously service-connected condition can be service-connected on a secondary basis.
- The VA can reassign the location of a disability grant (e.g., left vs. right side) even after 10 years, but if this results in a reduced evaluation, the VA must provide due process and propose the change.
- Periods when a Veteran is returned to Active Duty do not count toward the 10-year calculation.

This rule provides significant protection for Veterans against loss of service-connection after a decade, ensuring stability of their benefits.

**Sources:**
¹ [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)  
² [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

**Sources Cited:**
- [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)
- [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

### Question 67: RATING-22

**Query:** What is the 20 year rule?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.2s

**Answer:**

The 20 year rule, as described in 38 CFR 3.951, means that a Veteran's entitlement to a benefit or rating cannot be severed or reduced after 20 years, except in cases of fraud. However, there are some exceptions and special notes:

- If the VA clearly and erroneously granted service-connection for a disability involving a specific location (such as left vs. right or upper vs. lower), the VA can reassign the location of the grant even after 20 years. If this reassignment results in a reduced evaluation, the VA must provide due process and propose the change.
- Periods during which a Veteran is returned to Active Duty do not count toward the calculation of this 20-year protection.
- The pausing of Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) based on the need for aid and attendance is allowed when the Veteran is hospitalized at government expense.

**Sources:**
¹ [20 Years - Total Protection (38 CFR 3.951) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)  
² [20 Years - Total Protection (38 CFR 3.951) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

### Question 70: RATING-25

**Query:** How do I apply for TDIU?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 2.38s

**Answer:**

To apply for Total Disability based on Individual Unemployability (TDIU), you should submit a claim to the VA stating that your service-connected disabilities prevent you from holding down gainful employment. This is especially relevant if your Traumatic Brain Injury (TBI) or other service-connected conditions make you unable to maintain employment. You can start the application process by filing a claim specifically for TDIU benefits.

For more detailed guidance on filing, you can visit the TDIU page linked in the context, which provides comprehensive information on eligibility and the application process.

### Question 71: MENTAL-1

**Query:** How is PTSD rated by the VA?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 5.78s

**Answer:**

PTSD (Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder) is rated by the VA based on the severity of the totality of a Veteran's mental health symptoms, not on the specific diagnosis itself. This means that PTSD is rated similarly to other mental disorders, with a single rating assigned based on symptom severity regardless of the number of diagnoses a Veteran has¹.

For service-connection, PTSD requires an in-service stressor, which can include a wide range of traumatic events such as combat, assault, military sexual trauma, environmental disasters, and others. The Veteran's lay testimony may sometimes establish the occurrence of an in-service stressor under specific conditions, especially if supported by a VA psychiatrist or psychologist²³.

The VA rates mental disorders, including PTSD, by evaluating symptoms such as occupational and social impairment, reliability and productivity, panic attacks, memory impairment, judgment, mood disturbances, and social relationships. For example, a 50% rating corresponds to occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms like panic attacks more than once a week, difficulty understanding complex commands, impaired judgment, and disturbances of motivation and mood⁴.

Additionally, PTSD and TBI (Traumatic Brain Injury) symptoms often overlap, and the VA may lump the ratings together unless the symptoms can be clearly separated, in which case separate ratings may be assigned⁵.

The rating decision is based on all evidence submitted, including C&P exam reports, DBQs, nexus letters, buddy letters, personal statements, and treatment notes. The rating percentage assigned reflects the overall severity of symptoms, not just isolated symptoms⁶.

**Sources:**

¹ [Mental Disorder Ratings - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [PTSD Service-Connection - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
³ [3.  Concession of an In-Service Stressor (Part 4) - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
⁴ [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
⁵ [PTSD & TBI - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
⁶ [How a Rater Decides - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)
