### Question 72: MENTAL-2

**Query:** What symptoms qualify for PTSD rating?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 6.07s

**Answer:**

The symptoms that qualify for a PTSD rating, particularly at the 50% level, include:

- Occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity
- Flattened affect
- Circumstantial, circumlocutory, or stereotyped speech
- Panic attacks more than once a week
- Difficulty in understanding complex commands
- Impairment of short- and long-term memory (e.g., retention of only highly learned material, forgetting to complete tasks)
- Impaired judgment
- Impaired abstract thinking
- Disturbances of motivation and mood
- Difficulty in establishing and maintaining effective work and social relationships

Additional details about the veteran's abilities and symptoms at this rating level include:

- Mostly able to take care of themselves, though hygiene may not always be maintained
- Able to perform daily tasks such as driving, shopping, and household chores
- May occasionally be hospitalized
- Can work but may have difficulty maintaining employment for long periods
- May form social relationships, but they may be short-lived due to inability to actively participate
- Extreme lack of motivation and/or mood swings
- Difficulty expressing themselves or showing emotions, sometimes responding inappropriately to situations
- Two or more panic attacks per week
- Constant signs of severe anxiety
- Difficulty understanding complex directions
- Difficulty thinking logically and making judgments, possibly requiring a fiduciary

These symptoms reflect the severity and impact on occupational and social functioning that VA considers when rating PTSD.

### Question 73: MENTAL-3

**Query:** Can I get service connected for PTSD without combat?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 6.76s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get service-connected for PTSD without combat. PTSD service-connection requires an in-service stressor, but this stressor does not have to be related to direct combat exposure. Veterans may develop PTSD from a variety of non-combat stressors such as:

- Aircraft crashes  
- Assault or battery  
- Environmental disasters (earthquakes, fires, floods, etc.)  
- Harassment or military sexual trauma (MST) including sexual assault or harassment  
- Serving as a drone aircraft crew member  
- Serving in mortuary affairs  
- Stalking  
- Training accidents  
- Vehicular crashes  

Additionally, fear of hostile enemy forces is also recognized as a stressor, even if the Veteran was not in direct combat, but this does not include anticipation of future deployment or learning of a death away from the Veteran in a hostile location¹.

Non-combat stressors must be corroborated with credible supporting evidence, as a Veteran’s lay statement alone is generally insufficient for verification of the stressor unless it meets specific criteria such as combat, fear of hostile military or terrorist activity, or duties as a drone aircraft crew member confirmed by a VA psychiatrist or psychologist²³⁵.

Therefore, service connection for PTSD is possible without combat if the Veteran can establish an in-service stressor consistent with the circumstances of their service and supported by evidence.

**Sources:**
¹ [PTSD Service-Connection - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [3.  Concession of an In-Service Stressor (Part 4) - (claims_process)](Not available)

### Question 74: MENTAL-4

**Query:** What is MST (Military Sexual Trauma)?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 2.56s

**Answer:**

Military Sexual Trauma (MST) refers to experiences of sexual assault or sexual harassment that a veteran may have encountered during their military service. It is recognized as a type of in-service stressor that can lead to PTSD and is specifically mentioned as a cause for PTSD separate from combat-related stressors. Examples of MST include sexual assault, sexual harassment, rape, and related personal traumatic events occurring during military service¹.

### Question 77: MENTAL-7

**Query:** What is the 100% rating for mental health?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 4.67s

**Answer:**

The 100% rating for mental health indicates total occupational and social impairment due to severe symptoms. These symptoms include:

- Gross impairment in thought processes or communication
- Persistent delusions or hallucinations
- Grossly inappropriate behavior
- Persistent danger of hurting self or others
- Intermittent inability to perform activities of daily living (including maintenance of minimal personal hygiene)
- Disorientation to time or place
- Memory loss for names of close relatives, own occupation, or own name

Additional details about the veteran's functioning at this rating level include:

- Unable to care for themselves, requiring constant or near-constant hospitalization due to being a threat to themselves or others
- No ability to work (except possibly in a protected environment)
- Social relationships, if they exist, are one-way and directed toward the veteran; the veteran does not seek or encourage social relationships
- Symptoms such as constant risk to self or others (including suicidal tendencies), significant memory loss, constant severe anxiety, fear, paranoia, inappropriate behavior (drooling, mumbling, shouting), inability to differentiate reality from make-believe, inability to understand time or location, and inability to communicate, reason, or think logically¹³.

### Question 78: MENTAL-8

**Query:** Can I have multiple mental health ratings?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 2.58s

**Answer:**

No, you cannot have multiple mental health ratings. Veterans can only have one combined mental health rating regardless of how many mental disorders they have. The symptoms from all mental disorders are combined into a single rating. The only exception is if the Veteran has an eating disorder, in which case they can have one rating for their mental health disorders and a separate rating for the eating disorder¹⁴.

**Sources:**

### Question 79: MENTAL-9

**Query:** How is anxiety rated?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 8.57s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** Some specific numbers in this response could not be verified against my sources. Please verify key statistics with official VA documentation.

Anxiety is rated under the general rating formula for mental disorders, which assesses the severity of symptoms and their impact on occupational and social impairment. The rating levels are based on the degree of impairment caused by symptoms such as panic attacks, anxiety, mood changes, and social functioning.

Here are the key rating levels for anxiety:

- **10% Rating**: Occupational and social impairment due to mild or transient symptoms which decrease work efficiency and ability to perform occupational tasks only during periods of significant stress, or symptoms controlled by continuous medication. Symptoms include mild anxiety, depression, or other mood changes; rare mild panic attacks; occasional sleep disturbances. The veteran is always able to care for themselves and rarely has problems working or in social relationships¹⁴.

- **30% Rating**: Occupational and social impairment with occasional decrease in work efficiency and intermittent periods of inability to perform occupational tasks. Symptoms include depressed mood, anxiety, suspiciousness, panic attacks (weekly or less often), chronic sleep impairment, mild memory loss. The veteran can usually care for themselves but may occasionally be hospitalized, may occasionally be unable to work properly, and social relationships may be strained¹⁶.

- **50% Rating**: Occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as panic attacks more than once a week, difficulty understanding complex commands, impaired judgment, disturbances of mood, and difficulty maintaining effective work and social relationships. The veteran is mostly able to care for themselves but hygiene may not always be maintained, can work but may have difficulty staying employed long-term, and social relationships may be short-lived¹⁷.

- **70% and 100% Ratings**: These represent more severe occupational and social impairment with symptoms such as near-continuous panic or depression, suicidal ideation, gross impairment in thought processes, persistent danger of hurting self or others, and inability to perform activities of daily living. These higher ratings indicate severe disability but specific anxiety-related criteria are detailed under the general mental disorders rating formula¹³.

The veteran's formal diagnosis (e.g., generalized anxiety disorder, panic disorder) is not as important as the severity of symptoms when determining the rating. A single rating covers all mental health diagnoses combined¹⁵.

### Question 80: MENTAL-10

**Query:** How is depression rated?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 8.18s

**Answer:**

Depression is rated under the General Rating Formula for Mental Disorders based on the severity of symptoms and the resulting occupational and social impairment, rather than the specific diagnosis itself. The rating depends on how much the symptoms affect the veteran's ability to function in daily life, work, and social relationships.

The ratings are as follows:

- **100% rating**: Total occupational and social impairment due to symptoms such as gross impairment in thought processes, persistent delusions or hallucinations, grossly inappropriate behavior, persistent danger of hurting self or others, intermittent inability to perform activities of daily living, disorientation, or memory loss of close relatives or own identity.

- **70% rating**: Occupational and social impairment with deficiencies in most areas such as work, school, family relations, judgment, thinking, or mood. Symptoms may include suicidal ideation, obsessional rituals interfering with routine activities, illogical or obscure speech, near-continuous panic or depression affecting function, impaired impulse control, spatial disorientation, neglect of hygiene, difficulty adapting to stress, and inability to maintain effective relationships.

- **50% rating**: Occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as flattened affect, stereotyped speech, panic attacks more than once a week, difficulty understanding complex commands, impaired memory, impaired judgment and abstract thinking, disturbances of mood and motivation, and difficulty establishing and maintaining work and social relationships. The veteran is mostly able to care for themselves but may have hygiene issues and difficulty maintaining employment or relationships.

- Lower ratings (not fully detailed in the context) would correspond to less severe symptoms and impairment.

Importantly, the rating is based on the totality and severity of symptoms, not the diagnosis of depression itself. A veteran with depression can receive the same rating as one with PTSD or other mental disorders if the severity of symptoms is similar³.

¹ [General Rating Formula for Mental Disorders](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental#generalratingformulaformentaldisorders)

### Question 81: MENTAL-11

**Query:** What is occupational and social impairment?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 6.07s

**Answer:**

Occupational and social impairment refers to the reduction in a veteran's ability to function effectively in work and social settings due to mental health symptoms. It can range from mild to total impairment and affects areas such as work, school, family relations, judgment, thinking, mood, and social interactions.

- At 10% rating level, occupational and social impairment is due to mild or transient symptoms that decrease work efficiency and ability to perform occupational tasks only during periods of significant stress. Symptoms are mild and may be controlled by medication, with occasional social issues and rare problems at work¹.

- At 100% rating level, occupational and social impairment is total, characterized by gross impairment in thought processes or communication, persistent delusions or hallucinations, grossly inappropriate behavior, persistent danger of hurting self or others, inability to perform daily living activities, disorientation, and severe memory loss. The veteran is unable to care for themselves, unable to work, and has severely impaired social relationships³.

- The general rating formula describes occupational and social impairment as deficiencies in most areas such as work, school, family relations, judgment, thinking, or mood due to symptoms like suicidal ideation, panic or depression affecting function, impaired impulse control, neglect of hygiene, difficulty adapting to stress, and inability to maintain relationships¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [10% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
² [General Rating Formula for Mental Disorders - (rating_criteria)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental#generalratingformulaformentaldisorders)
³ [100% - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 83: MENTAL-13

**Query:** Do I need a diagnosis for PTSD claim?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 2.79s

**Answer:**

Yes, you need a diagnosis of PTSD for a PTSD service-connection claim. PTSD requires an in-service stressor and a diagnosis to establish service connection. The diagnosis is typically confirmed through a separate examination (DBQ) specific to PTSD, distinct from other mental health conditions¹. Additionally, the claimed stressor must be related to the Veteran’s service and supported by evidence such as lay testimony, military records, or medical examinations²⁴.

### Question 84: MENTAL-14

**Query:** Can I get PTSD rating for non-combat trauma?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 4.74s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get a PTSD rating for non-combat trauma. PTSD service-connection requires an in-service stressor, but this stressor does not have to be direct combat exposure. Examples of non-combat trauma that may qualify include:

- Aircraft crashes  
- Assault  
- Battery  
- Environmental disasters (earthquakes, fires, floods, etc)  
- Harassment  
- Military Sexual Trauma (MST)  
- Sexual assault  
- Sexual harassment  
- Rape  
- Mugging  
- Robbery  
- Serving as a Drone Aircraft Crew Member  
- Serving in Mortuary Affairs  
- Stalking  
- Training accidents  
- Vehicular crashes  

Fear of hostile enemy forces is also a qualifying stressor but does not include anticipation of future deployment or learning of the death of another person away from the veteran in a hostile area¹.

Therefore, non-combat traumas such as assault, sexual trauma, accidents, and other listed events can qualify for PTSD service-connection and rating.

### Question 85: MENTAL-15

**Query:** What evidence do I need for mental health claim?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 3.72s

**Answer:**

For a mental health claim, the evidence you need includes:

- Private medical records and non-VA hospital reports related to your claimed mental health condition or showing worsening of the disability. If you want VA to obtain these records, you must provide VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a.
- Newspaper articles that show you were personally involved in an event or that an event occurred during a time you were at a specific location.
- Buddy statements from family members, friends, clergy, law enforcement personnel, or fellow service members who can provide information about your claimed condition, how and when it happened, how it worsened, and its current effects.
- Supporting or personal statements to add context or detail to your evidence or to highlight important points in your records.
- For PTSD claims specifically, VA Form 21-0781 (Supporting Statements for Service-connection for PTSD) is generally required.

Note that the VA can access your VHA medical records themselves, so it is not necessary to upload them yourself, but you may provide additional evidence to support your claim.

### Question 86: MENTAL-16

**Query:** How do I prove my PTSD stressor?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 8.63s

**Answer:**

To prove your PTSD stressor for VA disability claims, you need to establish that the stressor occurred during your military service. Here are the key ways to prove your PTSD stressor:

- Submit a standard claim form for service connection of PTSD.
- Provide evidence of the stressor through:
  - VA Form 21-0781 (Supporting Statements for Service Connection for PTSD).
  - Lay statements or personal testimony describing the stressor.
  - Military records that show combat participation, service in areas consistent with hostile military or terrorist activity, or other relevant service circumstances.
  - Medical records or statements made to examiners that document the stressor or PTSD diagnosis.
- In some cases, such as combat or fear-based stressors, the VA may order examinations without specific stressor details, but if PTSD is diagnosed, the stressor must be verified as consistent with service places and circumstances.
- A Veteran’s lay testimony alone may establish an in-service stressor if:
  - PTSD was diagnosed in service and the stressor is related to that service, or
  - The stressor is related to combat engagement, experience as a prisoner of war, or fear of hostile military or terrorist activity, and a VA psychiatrist or psychologist confirms the stressor supports the PTSD diagnosis and symptoms.
- The stressor must be consistent with the circumstances, conditions, or hardships of service or the places and types of service.
- The VA will review various types of evidence to verify the stressor, including:
  - After action reports, buddy letters, combat awards on your DD-214, command chronologies, diaries, daily staff journals, personal statements, police or insurance reports, private medical records, service treatment records, unit histories, and more.

This evidence helps corroborate the occurrence of the stressor during your service and supports your PTSD claim³⁵.

² [3. Concession of an In-Service Stressor (Part 6) (Part 1)](Not available)  
³ [3. Concession of an In-Service Stressor (Part 4)](Not available)  
⁵ [Evidence that VA looks at to Verify In-Service Stressors](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 87: MENTAL-17

**Query:** Can I get 100% for PTSD alone?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 3.94s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can receive a 100% disability rating for PTSD alone if your condition results in total occupational and social impairment. The criteria for a 100% rating include symptoms such as:

- Gross impairment in thought processes or communication
- Persistent delusions or hallucinations
- Grossly inappropriate behavior
- Persistent danger of hurting self or others
- Intermittent inability to perform activities of daily living (including maintenance of minimal personal hygiene)
- Disorientation to time or place
- Memory loss for names of close relatives, own occupation, or own name

Additionally, the veteran would be unable to care for themselves, require constant or near-constant hospitalization due to being a threat to themselves or others, have no ability to work, and have severely impaired social relationships¹.

Therefore, if your PTSD symptoms meet these criteria, you can be rated at 100% for PTSD alone.

**Sources:**

### Question 88: MENTAL-18

**Query:** What is the difference between 50% and 70% PTSD?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 7.79s

**Answer:**

The difference between 50% and 70% PTSD ratings lies in the severity and impact of symptoms on occupational and social functioning.

- **50% PTSD rating** is characterized by:
  - Occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity.
  - Symptoms such as flattened affect, circumstantial or stereotyped speech, panic attacks more than once a week, difficulty understanding complex commands, impaired memory, judgment, and abstract thinking.
  - Disturbances in motivation and mood.
  - Difficulty establishing and maintaining effective work and social relationships.
  - The veteran is mostly able to care for themselves but hygiene may not always be maintained.
  - Able to perform daily tasks like driving, shopping, and household chores.
  - May occasionally be hospitalized.
  - Can work but may have difficulty maintaining employment for long periods.
  - May form relationships but they may be short-lived due to inability to be an active participant.
  - Symptoms include extreme lack of motivation, mood swings, difficulty expressing emotions, inappropriate emotional responses, 2 or more panic attacks a week, severe anxiety, difficulty understanding complex directions, poor judgment, and may require a fiduciary¹.

- **70% PTSD rating** is not explicitly detailed in the provided context, but from the example in the TBI rating context, a 70% PTSD rating is associated with more severe symptoms that likely cause greater occupational and social impairment than the 50% rating. The 70% rating implies a higher level of disability and symptom severity than 50%, but exact criteria are not provided in the sources.

Therefore, the key difference is that 50% rating reflects moderate symptoms with some functional impairment but the ability to work and care for oneself with difficulty, while 70% rating indicates more severe symptoms and impairment, though the exact criteria for 70% are not given in the provided context.

**Sources:**

¹ [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 89: MENTAL-19

**Query:** How does the VA evaluate mental health?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 5.46s

**Answer:**

The VA evaluates mental health disorders based on the severity of the totality of a Veteran's symptoms rather than the specific formal diagnosis. A Veteran receives a single rating for their mental health condition(s), regardless of the number of diagnoses they have, except in the case of an eating disorder, which is rated separately from other mental health disorders¹³.

The rating is determined by a rater who reviews all submitted evidence, including Compensation & Pension (C&P) exam reports, Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs), nexus letters, buddy statements, personal statements, supporting statements for PTSD claims, private and VA treatment notes, and Vet Center notes (with permission). The rater assigns a rating percentage that most closely matches the overall severity of the Veteran's symptoms, considering the totality of evidence rather than isolated symptoms².

For example, a 50% rating corresponds to occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as flattened affect, panic attacks more than once a week, impaired judgment, and difficulty maintaining effective work and social relationships. The Veteran may be mostly able to care for themselves but may have difficulty sustaining employment and forming lasting relationships⁴.

Veterans are encouraged to be completely honest during their C&P examination without exaggerating or fabricating symptoms, as the rating depends on an accurate assessment of symptom severity³.

**Sources:**

¹ [Mental Disorder Ratings - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [How a Rater Decides - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
³ [Mental Disorders - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
⁴ [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Mental Disorder Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 91: PHYSICAL-1

**Query:** How is back pain rated by the VA?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 4.98s

**Answer:**

Back pain is generally rated by the VA based on the limitation of Range of Motion (ROM) of the spine rather than the specific diagnosis of the back condition. The rating focuses on either the neck (cervical spine) or the rest of the back (thoracolumbar spine). Key points include:

- The examiner and rater must consider flare-ups and estimate the worst impairment even if the examination is not conducted on the worst day.
- The rating is based on measured forward flexion and combined ROM degrees.
- Veterans can receive separate ratings for the neck and the back but only one rating per section of the spine, even if multiple conditions affect that section.
- Pain with motion can also be considered, but for the back specifically, the rating is primarily based on ROM limitations.

The exact rating percentages correspond to specific ROM measurements, for example, forward flexion of the back measuring 90° or more results in a 0% rating, while more severe limitations correspond to higher ratings (detailed tables are in the source but truncated here)¹⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [(Back and Neck) - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/spine)  
⁴ [DC 5235 - through 5242 Ratings Based Off Limitations of Range of Motion - (rating_table)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/nervesystem#5235)

**Sources Cited:**
- [(Back and Neck)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/spine)
- [Ratings Involving the Lower Back and Legs](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/spine)
- [Arthritis of the Spine](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/spine)

### Question 93: PHYSICAL-3

**Query:** How are shoulder conditions rated?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 5.4s

**Answer:**

The context provided does not contain detailed information on how shoulder conditions are rated in general. However, there is specific information about shoulder replacement (prosthesis) under Diagnostic Code (DC) 5051:

- After a shoulder replacement procedure, any other musculoskeletal evaluations for that joint (such as ankylosis or range of motion) will be discontinued from the date of the procedure.
- A temporary 100% disability rating is granted following the surgery, which lasts for 1 year plus an additional month (total 13 months) due to 38 CFR 4.30.
- After the temporary 100% rating ends, the Veteran can file for an increase if symptoms warrant a higher evaluation.
- If the condition could be rated higher under ankylosis, that higher rating will replace the shoulder replacement evaluation.
- Medical evidence must show the date of admission, discharge, surgery, and the procedure performed.
- If the procedure was for a non-service-connected condition, VA will obtain a medical opinion to determine if it is a progression of the service-connected condition.

Examples of how the temporary 100% rating period is calculated are provided, showing the start date as the date of admission for surgery and the end date as 13 months later, accounting for the bonus month under 38 CFR 4.30.

No other specific rating percentages or criteria for shoulder conditions are included in the provided context.

**Sources:**
¹ [DC 5051 - Shoulder Replacement - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/shoulder#5051)

### Question 94: PHYSICAL-4

**Query:** What is limited range of motion?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 2.39s

**Answer:**

Limited range of motion refers to the reduced ability to move a joint or part of the body normally. It means that the joint cannot move through its full, typical range of motion due to disability or injury¹.

### Question 95: PHYSICAL-5

**Query:** How is sleep apnea rated?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 3.96s

**Answer:**

Sleep apnea is rated by the VA based on the severity of symptoms and the need for breathing assistance devices. The rating criteria are as follows:

- 0%: There is a diagnosis of sleep apnea but no symptoms.
- 30%: The veteran experiences persistent sleepiness during the daytime or does not feel rested after sleeping.
- 50%: The veteran requires the use of a breathing assistance device such as a CPAP machine or other positive airway pressure devices (e.g., APAP, BiPAP), nasopharyngeal appliances, oral appliances, or implanted genioglossal nerve stimulation devices.

It is important to note that the VA requires a sleep study to grant service connection for sleep apnea. Home sleep studies are acceptable if clinically appropriate and evaluated by a competent medical provider. Also, while the use of a CPAP machine is required to qualify for the 50% rating, the veteran is not required to use it continuously to maintain the rating¹.

Upper Airway Resistance Syndrome (UARS) is not considered a ratable disability and does not meet the criteria for sleep apnea for compensation purposes².

**Sources:**
¹ [DC 6847 - Sleep Apnea - (rating_table)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/airsystem#6847)  
² [Upper Airway Resistance Syndrome (UARS) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/airsystem)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Sleep Apnea](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/airsystem#6847)
- [Upper Airway Resistance Syndrome (UARS)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/airsystem)

**Confidence:** 0.5998034477233887
**Chunks Retrieved:** 2

### Question 96: PHYSICAL-6

**Query:** What rating do I get for CPAP use?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 2.26s

**Answer:**

If you are prescribed a CPAP machine for sleep apnea, you qualify for a 50% disability rating. It is important to note that while it is recommended for your health to use the CPAP, you do not have to use it to maintain this 50% rating. The VA requires a sleep study to grant service connection for sleep apnea, and home sleep studies are acceptable under certain conditions¹.

**Sources:**

### Question 97: PHYSICAL-7

**Query:** How is hypertension rated?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 4.03s

**Answer:**

Hypertension (high blood pressure) is rated based on specific criteria including blood pressure readings taken two or more times on at least three different days. Veterans must have a proper diagnosis of hypertension to be rated.

Important notes include:
- Veterans with hypertensive heart disease can receive a separate rating for hypertension.
- Veterans with hypertension due to aortic insufficiency or hyperthyroidism will not receive a separate rating for hypertension.
- Presumptive conditions apply if diagnosed within a year of separation with at least 10% severity, or if exposed to Agent Orange, or if a POW with at least 10% severity at some point.
- Quasi-presumptive service connection applies for hypertension developing in veterans with certain service-connected amputations.

The exact rating percentages and criteria for hypertension are detailed in the rating table on the source, but the context provided does not include the full rating table details.

Additionally, related heart conditions such as right-sided heart failure, right ventricular hypertrophy, and pulmonary hypertension are rated at 100% each, but these are separate from the hypertension rating itself¹³.

**Sources:**
¹ [DC 7101 - High Blood Pressure - (rating_table)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/bloodtubes#7101)

### Question 99: PHYSICAL-9

**Query:** How is hearing loss rated?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 7.32s

**Answer:**

Hearing loss is rated by the VA using a combination of puretone audiometry and speech discrimination test results for both ears together, not individually. The process involves the following steps:

- The audiologist conducts two tests:
  - Puretone audiometry test: measures hearing threshold levels at various frequencies.
  - Speech discrimination test: measures the ability to understand spoken words, scored as a percentage.

- Each ear's results are assigned a Roman numeral (I through XI) based on a table that combines puretone threshold averages and speech discrimination percentages. Higher numerals indicate worse hearing loss.

- Using the Roman numerals for the worst ear and the best ear, a rating table is applied to determine the overall disability rating percentage. The rating ranges from 0% to 100%, with specific percentages assigned based on the combination of hearing loss severity in both ears.

- To qualify for any rating (above 0%), the hearing loss must meet at least one of these criteria:
  - Auditory threshold of 40 decibels or greater at any of the frequencies 500, 1000, 2000, 3000, or 4000 Hertz.
  - Auditory thresholds of 26 decibels or greater at at least three of those frequencies.
  - Speech recognition scores less than 94 percent.

- If one ear is service-connected and the other is not, the VA may consider the non-service-connected ear as service-connected for rating purposes if:
  - The non-service-connected ear was not due to willful misconduct.
  - The non-service-connected ear has hearing loss for VA purposes.
  - The service-connected ear rates at least 10% by itself.

- If only puretone testing was done, a separate puretone-only table is used to assign the Roman numeral for each ear.

- If an ear is not service-connected, it is considered to be in perfect working order (100%) for rating purposes unless special conditions apply.

- Hearing aids must be removed before testing.

This combined approach ensures the rating reflects the overall impact of hearing loss on both ears, not just one ear individually³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Rating Table - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)  
² [DC 6100 - Hearing Loss - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears#6100)  
³ [Puretone and Speech Discrimination Table - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Rating Table](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)
- [Hearing Loss](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears#6100)
- [Puretone and Speech Discrimination Table](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)
- [Rating Schedule for the Ear](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)
- [Puretone only table](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)
- [Rating Schedule for Taste and Smell](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)

**Confidence:** 0.6365875005722046

### Question 100: PHYSICAL-10

**Query:** What is the rating for migraines?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 4.52s

**Answer:**

The VA rates migraine headaches based on the severity and frequency of prostrating attacks. The key term is "prostrating," meaning the headache is so severe that the Veteran's day is effectively ended when it starts, possibly requiring medication, isolation, or medical attention. If the headaches are not prostrating, no compensable rating is given.

The rating percentages depend on the frequency of prostrating attacks:

- 10% rating: 1 to 2 prostrating attacks per month, on average.
- 30% rating: 3 to 4 prostrating attacks per month, on average.
- 50% rating: 4 or more prostrating attacks per month, on average.

It is important for Veterans to document the frequency and severity of their headaches, for example, by using headache tracking apps or personal/buddy statements.

Additionally, migraine headaches are presumptive if diagnosed within 1 year of separation and the severity warranted at least a 10% evaluation within that time.

If exposed to Gulf environmental hazards, the condition is considered a MUCMI (Medical Unexplained Chronic Multisymptom Illness).

**Sources:**
¹ [DC 8100 - Migraine Headaches - (rating_table)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental#8100)

- [Rating Schedule for The Central Nervous System](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)

### Question 101: PHYSICAL-11

**Query:** How is diabetes rated?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 4.52s

**Answer:**

Diabetes mellitus is rated by the VA based on whether the pancreas produces enough insulin (Type 1) or the body's response to insulin (Type 2). The rating considers the need for insulin, a restricted diet, and regulation of activities, especially when diabetes has caused episodes of ketoacidosis resulting in hospitalization. Use of non-insulin injectable medications is treated as oral hypoglycemic agents and not equivalent to insulin treatment for rating purposes.

Additionally, diabetes may be considered a presumptive condition if diagnosed within one year of separation and warrants at least a 10% evaluation, or if the veteran was exposed to Agent Orange and the severity ever warranted at least a 10% evaluation.

Regulation of activities means avoiding strenuous activity to prevent hypoglycemic episodes, but voluntary avoidance or prescribed exercise alone does not meet the criteria for rating purposes¹.

For diabetic retinopathy, which is damage to the retina caused by diabetes, the rating is based on the Visual Impairment Rating System or on Incapacitating Episodes, whichever gives the highest rating².

**Sources:**
¹ [DC 7913 - Diabetes Mellitus - (rating_table)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/endsystem#7913)  
² [DC 6040 - Diabetic Retinopathy - (condition_definition)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/eyes#6040)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Diabetes Mellitus](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/endsystem#7913)
- [Diabetic Retinopathy](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/eyes#6040)

**Confidence:** 0.6128118634223938

### Question 102: PHYSICAL-12

**Query:** What is the rating for erectile dysfunction?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 2.51s

**Answer:**

The rating for erectile dysfunction (ED) is 0%. This rating is assigned for difficulty or inability to achieve or maintain an erection. It is important to note that the ability to sometimes achieve an erection or impregnate someone does not prevent service connection or entitlement to SMC K. Additionally, if ED is the result of diabetes mellitus, a separate rating for ED is not given, but the veteran is still entitled to SMC K¹.

### Question 103: PHYSICAL-13

**Query:** How are scars rated?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 12.43s

**Answer:**

Scars are rated by the VA based on several factors including their location, size, characteristics, and associated symptoms such as pain or instability. The rating process involves different diagnostic codes depending on the type and location of the scars:

- **Scars NOT involving the head, face, or neck** are divided into six body areas (torso front, back, left arm, right arm, left leg, right leg). Each area can be rated separately unless combining scar sizes from all areas results in a higher rating. Deep and superficial scars in the same area receive separate ratings. If scars cause range of motion issues, a separate rating for that impairment can be granted. Bilateral factor consideration applies unless the rating is less than 10% or specific to head, face, or neck scars¹.

- **Painful or unstable scars (DC 7804)**: If scars are painful when touched (self-reports are insufficient) or unstable (frequent loss of skin covering), the veteran can receive a rating for the scar plus an additional 10% if at least one scar is both painful and unstable. Ratings are 10% for 1-2 scars, 20% for 3-4 scars, and 30% for 5 or more scars².

- **General skin conditions (including scars)** are rated based on lesion coverage and treatment requirements. Ratings range from 0% (lesions cover less than 5% of the body or require only topical treatment) to 60% (lesions cover more than 40% of the body or require continuous systemic therapy)³.

- **Scars involving the head, face, or neck (DC 7800)** are rated based on visible or palpable disfigurement, including scar length, width, attachment to underlying tissue, abnormal skin color or texture, and loss of underlying soft tissue. Multiple scars cannot be combined to meet width requirements. Separate ratings can be given for nerve damage or loss of eye/ear associated with the scars⁵.

- **Superficial scars (DC 7802)** affect only the skin and not underlying tissues. A 10% rating is given if superficial scars cover 144 square inches (929 cm²) or more⁷.

- **All other scars (DC 7805)** are rated under the diagnostic code that best captures the impairment⁴.

In summary, scars are rated based on their location, size, depth, pain or instability, and associated functional impairments, with specific diagnostic codes and criteria applied accordingly.

**Sources:**
¹ [Scars NOT Involving the Head, Face, or Neck](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin)  
² [DC 7804 - Painful or Unstable Scars](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin#7804)  
³ [General Rating Formula For The Skin](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin)  
⁴ [DC 7805 - All other Scars](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin#7805)  
⁵ [DC 7800 - Scars and Disfigurement Involving the Head, Face, or Neck](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin#7800)  
⁷ [DC 7802 - Superficial Scars](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin#7802)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Scars NOT Involving the Head, Face, or Neck](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin)
- [Painful or Unstable Scars](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin#7804)
- [General Rating Formula For The Skin](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin)
- [All other Scars](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin#7805)

### Question 105: PHYSICAL-15

**Query:** How is GERD rated?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 4.98s

**Answer:**

Gastroesophageal Reflux Disease (GERD) is rated under Diagnostic Code (DC) 7206 with the following criteria:

- 0% rating: Without daily symptoms or requirement for daily medications.
- 10% rating: Esophageal stricture(s) that requires daily medications to control difficulty swallowing. A 10% evaluation is appropriate even if there is no evidence of esophageal stricture(s) but daily medication is required to control other symptoms associated with GERD.
- 30% rating: Esophageal stricture(s) that cause difficulty swallowing requiring dilation no more than 2 times a year.
- 50% rating: Recurrent or refractory esophageal stricture(s) causing difficulty swallowing (details truncated in source).

Important notes:
- GERD cannot be rated separately from difficulty swallowing (DC 7203).
- The condition must be medically documented, and if strictures are the basis, documented by barium swallow, CT scan, or esophagogastroduodenoscopy.
- GERD is not considered a functional gastrointestinal disorder.
- The VA may award evaluations even if all symptoms are not present, based on 38 CFR 4.7.

In summary, GERD ratings depend largely on the presence and severity of esophageal strictures and the need for daily medication or dilation procedures to control symptoms¹³².

**Sources:**
¹ [DC 7206 - Gastroesophageal Reflux Disease - (rating_table)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/digsystem#7206)

³ [DC 7203 - Difficulty Swallowing - (rating_table)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/digsystem#7203)

### Question 106: PHYSICAL-16

**Query:** What is the rating for IBS?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 5.21s

**Answer:**

The rating for Irritable Bowel Syndrome (IBS) is based on the frequency of abdominal pain related to bowel movements and the presence of other symptoms such as distension, changes in stool frequency, form, and passage. The ratings are as follows:

- 10%: Abdominal pain related to pooping at least once during the last 3 months; and 2 or more reported distension symptoms.
- 20%: Abdominal pain related to pooping at least 3 days per month during the last 3 months; and 2 or more reported distension symptoms.
- 30%: Abdominal pain related to pooping at least 1 day per week during the last 3 months; and 2 or more of the following: change in stool frequency, change in stool form, altered stool passage (straining and/or urgency), mucorrhea, or abdominal distension¹.

Additionally, IBS is a presumptive condition for veterans exposed to Gulf environmental hazards or who were POWs for at least 30 days and had a severity warranting at least a 10% evaluation at any time¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [DC 7319 - Irritable Bowel Syndrome - (rating_table)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/digsystem#7319)

### Question 109: PHYSICAL-19

**Query:** How are TBI symptoms rated?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 5.05s

**Answer:**

TBI symptoms are rated based on the severity of the residual symptoms a Veteran has in 10 specific areas of impairment. The VA evaluates the Veteran in each of these areas but assigns only one singular TBI rating based on the area with the highest level of impairment, unless it is more beneficial to separate symptoms into individual ratings. However, a Veteran will not receive 10 individual ratings for TBI symptoms¹.

If the examiner diagnoses a TBI but notes no residual symptoms, a 0% rating is granted if service-connection is warranted¹.

Additionally, the severity of the TBI itself is categorized as mild, moderate, or severe based on criteria such as brain imaging results, duration of loss of consciousness, disorientation, amnesia, and Glasgow Coma Scale scores. Meeting just one criterion in a category qualifies the TBI for that severity level³.

There is also an important consideration regarding overlapping symptoms with PTSD. If the examiner cannot separate TBI symptoms from PTSD symptoms, the ratings may be lumped together. However, if the symptoms can be clearly separated by a specialist, the Veteran can receive separate ratings for TBI and PTSD⁵.

Finally, the VA is prohibited from assigning multiple ratings for the same symptoms (pyramiding), so care is taken to avoid rating the same symptom twice, even if it appears under different conditions such as vertigo or headaches⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [How TBI is Rated](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/TBI)
³ [Severity of TBI](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/TBI)
⁵ [PTSD & TBI](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
⁶ [Pyramiding](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**
- [How TBI is Rated](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/TBI)
- [TBI Rating Example](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/TBI)
- [Severity of TBI](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/TBI)
- [Traumatic Brain Injury (TBI)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cns)
- [PTSD & TBI](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [Pyramiding](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)
- [Common Secondary Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)

**Confidence:** 0.7157763838768005
**Chunks Retrieved:** 7

### Question 110: PHYSICAL-20

**Query:** What is the rating for chronic fatigue?

**Category:** PHYSICAL

**Response Time:** 2.9s

**Answer:**

The context does not provide specific percentage ratings for chronic fatigue syndrome (CFS). It describes the diagnostic criteria and important notes for CFS, such as:

- The condition must be diagnosed by excluding all other clinical conditions that could cause similar symptoms.
- There must be new onset of debilitating fatigue severe enough to reduce daily activity to less than 50% of the usual level for at least 6 months.
- At least 6 specific symptoms must be present (such as acute onset, fatigue lasting 24 hours or longer after exercise, muscle aches, headaches, etc.).
- Incapacitation exists only when a licensed physician prescribes bed rest and treatment.

However, no specific VA disability rating percentages or rating criteria are given for chronic fatigue syndrome in the provided information¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [DC 6354 - Chronic Fatigue Syndrome](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental#6354)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 111: SECONDARY-1

**Query:** What is a secondary condition?

**Category:** SECONDARY

**Response Time:** 4.13s

**Answer:**

A secondary condition is a medical condition that develops as a result of or is caused by a primary service-connected disability. For example, spinal issues can lead to nerve-based secondary conditions, which are often easier to grant service connection for if linked to the spine. Secondary conditions can include a wide range of health problems such as eye conditions, mental health disorders, digestive issues, heart problems, kidney issues, nerve problems, and others. Even if a secondary condition is rated at 0%, it will still be service-connected but may not receive a separate disability rating³⁴⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [Common Secondary Conditions - (related_conditions)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)

⁵ [Common Secondary Conditions - (related_conditions)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)

### Question 112: SECONDARY-2

**Query:** Can I get service connected for a secondary condition?

**Category:** SECONDARY

**Response Time:** 6.42s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get service-connected for a secondary condition if it is caused or aggravated by a service-connected primary condition. Secondary conditions related to mental disorders, traumatic brain injury (TBI), spinal issues, and other service-connected disabilities may be eligible for service connection. However, the secondary condition must have a proper diagnosis and medical evidence linking it to the primary service-connected condition.

Some common secondary conditions that may be service-connected include:

- Erectile dysfunction, eye issues, heart conditions, high blood pressure, kidney issues, mental disorders, nerve issues, skin ulcers, and stroke¹  
- Secondary disabilities caused by mental disorders or medications used to treat them, such as sleep apnea, irritable bowel syndrome, GERD, temporomandibular disorder, and erectile dysfunction²  
- Secondary conditions related to TBI, such as headaches, hearing loss, hormone deficiencies, mental health conditions, Parkinson's disease, scars, seizures, tinnitus, and vision impairment³  
- Nerve-based secondary conditions linked to spinal issues, which may have favorable presumptive consideration and possibly earlier effective dates⁴  
- Mental health conditions and migraines⁵  
- Tinnitus⁶  
- Hearing loss⁷  

Note that if the secondary condition rates 0%, it will not receive a separate rating but will still be service-connected¹. Also, pyramiding (rating the same symptom twice) is not allowed³.

### Question 113: SECONDARY-3

**Query:** What conditions are secondary to PTSD?

**Category:** SECONDARY

**Response Time:** 4.08s

**Answer:**

Conditions that can be secondary to PTSD (Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder), which is a mental health condition, include various mental health-related disabilities and other secondary conditions caused by the mental disorder or its treatment. Specifically, secondary conditions related to mental health disorders like PTSD may include:

- Erectile Dysfunction (ED) and Female Sexual Arousal Disorder (FSAD)  
- Irritable Bowel Syndrome (IBS)  
- Gastroesophageal Reflux Disease (GERD)  
- Sleep Apnea (SA)  
- Temporomandibular Disorder (TMD, TMJ)  
- Depression  
- Fatigue  

Additionally, medications used to treat mental disorders like PTSD may cause secondary disabilities, which can also be service-connected if properly diagnosed¹³⁴⁶.

### Question 116: SECONDARY-6

**Query:** What is secondary to diabetes?

**Category:** SECONDARY

**Response Time:** 5.21s

**Answer:**

Secondary conditions related to diabetes include:

- Erectile dysfunction  
- Eye issues such as retinopathy, blindness, cataracts, glaucoma, double vision, keratitis or keratomalacia (due to Vitamin A deficiency)  
- Heart conditions including coronary artery disease, heart attack, atherosclerosis, congestive heart failure, hyperthyroid heart disease  
- High blood pressure  
- Kidney issues such as nephropathy, decreased renal function, kidney stones  
- Mental disorders such as dementia and depression  
- Nerve issues including neuropathy (which can also be due to Vitamin E deficiency)  
- Skin ulcers and residual scars  
- Stroke  
- Bone fractures  
- Headaches  
- Peripheral artery disease  
- Digestive conditions  
- Vitamin or mineral deficiencies (avitaminosis) such as deficiencies in Vitamin A, B, C, D, E, or K which can cause various complications like prolonged clotting  

These conditions are commonly secondary to diabetes and may be service-connected as secondary conditions in VA claims³⁴⁵⁶⁷.

### Question 118: SECONDARY-8

**Query:** What is secondary to back pain?

**Category:** SECONDARY

**Response Time:** 9.36s

**Answer:**

Secondary conditions related to back pain, especially those with a nerve component, are commonly recognized by the VA. These secondary conditions can include:

- Nerve-based secondary conditions linked to the spine, which are considered quasi-presumptive and are generally granted unless there is conflicting evidence¹.
- Bladder and bowel control issues².
- Depression².
- Dizziness or vertigo².
- Fatigue².
- Nerve issues in both lower and upper extremities².
- Vision problems² ⁷.
- Bone fractures³ ⁷.
- Diabetes mellitus³.
- Erectile dysfunction³.
- Headaches and migraines³ ⁴ ⁵.
- High blood pressure³.
- Mental disorders and mental health conditions³ ⁴ ⁵.
- Hearing loss and tinnitus⁵ ⁶.
- Hormone deficiencies⁵.
- Parkinson’s disease⁵.
- Scars⁵.
- Seizures⁵.
- Bone loss of the skull⁶.
- Facial nerve paralysis⁶.
- Decreased renal function⁷.
- Digestive conditions⁷.
- Heart issues⁷.
- Kidney stones⁷.

Additionally, if the secondary condition has a nerve component linked to the spine, it may receive an earlier effective date up to a year before the claim was initiated if supported by medical evidence¹.

### Question 121: SECONDARY-11

**Query:** Can I file for secondary conditions later?

**Category:** SECONDARY

**Response Time:** 4.11s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can file for secondary conditions later. Secondary conditions are those that develop or are aggravated by a service-connected condition, including those granted under 38 USC 1151 (VA negligence) claims. You can claim conditions that developed or worsened due to your service-connected condition at any time¹.

Additionally, if you have a nerve-based secondary condition linked to a spinal condition, it is generally easier to get service connection for that secondary condition, and you may even receive an earlier effective date if there is medical evidence showing the condition existed before you filed the claim².

It is important to have a proper diagnosis before filing a claim for secondary conditions, and sometimes a medical opinion (IMO) may be necessary to establish the connection³.

**Sources:**

¹ [38 USC 1151 Claims (VA Negligence) - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#faq)  
² [Common Secondary Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)  
³ [Common Secondary Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)

### Question 122: SECONDARY-12

**Query:** What evidence do I need for secondary claim?

**Category:** SECONDARY

**Response Time:** 4.56s

**Answer:**

For a secondary claim, you need to submit new and relevant evidence that directly addresses the reason your claim was previously denied. This evidence can include:

- Medical records showing a diagnosis or worsening of the secondary condition.
- Evidence linking the secondary condition to the primary service-connected disability (a new theory of service connection).
- Independent medical opinions or nexus letters that establish the connection between the primary and secondary conditions.
- Buddy statements or personal statements that provide additional context or support for your claim.
- Documentation of being seen by a doctor for the secondary condition.
- If you missed a VA exam previously, a statement of willingness to attend a new exam.

Additionally, if your secondary condition is nerve-based and linked to a spinal condition, it is considered a quasi-presumptive condition, which may facilitate grant of the claim unless there is conflicting evidence¹⁵.

Remember, you have 1 year from the decision on your claim to file a supplemental claim with new and relevant evidence to potentially keep your back pay¹³⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
⁵ [Common Secondary Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)  
⁶ [Supplemental Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#supplementalclaim)

**Sources Cited:**
- [2\. Evidence Submission](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)
- [38 USC 1151 Claims (VA Negligence) - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#faq)
- [Supplemental Claim [(20-0995)](http://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0995-ARE.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)

### Question 124: SECONDARY-14

**Query:** What is aggravation of a secondary condition?

**Category:** SECONDARY

**Response Time:** 4.21s

**Answer:**

Aggravation of a secondary condition refers to a situation where a service-connected condition worsens an existing condition that developed after service (a post-service condition). For the VA to consider this aggravation, it must establish two things:

- The level of severity of the condition before aggravation.
- The level of severity of the condition after aggravation.

You must provide medical evidence showing the severity of the condition before aggravation or as soon as possible after aggravation but before the current severity is documented. The VA will then obtain a medical opinion to determine if the service-connected condition worsened the existing condition beyond its natural progression at least as likely as not. If the opinion is favorable, the condition can be service-connected due to aggravation¹.

There are two types of service-connection by aggravation:

1. Aggravation of a preexisting (pre-service) condition by military service.
2. Aggravation of an existing (post-service) condition by a service-connected condition².

In the context of secondary conditions, these are conditions that result from or are caused by a service-connected disability. If a service-connected condition aggravates such a secondary condition, it may be considered aggravation of a secondary condition, requiring the same proof of worsening beyond natural progression.

**Sources:**
¹ [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)  
² [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)
- [Common Secondary Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)
- [Service-Connection by Aggravation - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#references)
- [Common Secondary Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)
- [Common [Secondary Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)
- [Common Secondary Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)

### Question 125: SECONDARY-15

**Query:** Can migraines be secondary to TBI?

**Category:** SECONDARY

**Response Time:** 1.77s

**Answer:**

Yes, migraines can be secondary to a Traumatic Brain Injury (TBI). Migraines are listed as a common secondary condition that can be service-connected if the TBI is shown to be the cause. However, the Veteran cannot have the same symptom rated twice due to VA's anti-pyramiding rules¹.

### Question 126: PRESUMPTIVE-1

**Query:** What is a presumptive condition?

**Category:** PRESUMPTIVE

**Response Time:** 3.19s

**Answer:**

A presumptive condition is a health condition that the VA automatically assumes was caused by military service, without the veteran needing to prove the connection. This presumption is established by law or regulation. To qualify, the veteran must meet specific service requirements related to the presumption. Essentially, if you have a presumptive condition, you do not need to prove that your service caused the condition; the VA presumes it did¹³.

**Sources:**
¹ [What does it mean to have a presumptive condition?](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#whatdoesitmeantohaveapresumptivecondition)  
³ [What does it mean to have a presumptive condition for toxic exposure?](https://www.va.gov/resources/the-pact-act-and-your-va-benefits/#whatdoesitmeantohaveapresumptiveconditionfortoxicexposure)

### Question 127: PRESUMPTIVE-2

**Category:** PRESUMPTIVE

**Presumptive Cancers:**
- Brain cancer
- Gastrointestinal cancer of any type
- Glioblastoma
- Head cancer of any type
- Kidney cancer
- Lymphoma of any type
- Melanoma
- Neck cancer of any type
- Pancreatic cancer
- Reproductive cancer of any type
- Respiratory (breathing-related) cancer of any type

- Asthma diagnosed after service
- Chronic bronchitis
- Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD)
- Chronic rhinitis
- Chronic sinusitis
- Constrictive bronchiolitis or obliterative bronchiolitis
- Emphysema
- Granulomatous disease
- Interstitial lung disease (ILD)
- Pleuritis
- Pulmonary fibrosis
- Sarcoidosis

These presumptions apply to Gulf War era and post-9/11 veterans who were exposed to burn pits or other toxic substances during service¹.

### Question 128: PRESUMPTIVE-3

**Query:** What is Agent Orange presumptive?

**Category:** PRESUMPTIVE

**Response Time:** 6.3s

**Answer:**

Agent Orange presumptive refers to certain diseases that the Department of Veterans Affairs (VA) recognizes as being caused by exposure to Agent Orange, a herbicide used during the Vietnam War. Veterans who were exposed to Agent Orange and developed these diseases within one year of last exposure are eligible for presumptive service connection for these conditions, meaning they do not have to prove the disease was caused by their service.

The specific presumptive diseases related to Agent Orange exposure are:

- Chloracne or other acneform disease consistent with chloracne
- Early-onset peripheral neuropathy
- Porphyria cutanea tarda

These conditions must have reached at least 10% severity within one year of the last possible exposure to Agent Orange to qualify for presumptive status¹.

Additionally, eligibility for presumptive status depends on where and when the Veteran served, including service in locations such as the Republic of Vietnam, Thailand, Laos, Cambodia, Guam, American Samoa, Korean DMZ, Johnston Atoll, and service on certain Air Force units with C-123 aircraft exposed to Agent Orange during specific time frames².

If a Veteran can show that exposure was as likely as not in certain non-presumptive locations where Agent Orange was stored, destroyed, or tested, the VA may concede exposure and consider presumptive status³.

In summary, Agent Orange presumptive means the VA presumes that certain diseases diagnosed in Veterans who served in specific locations and time periods were caused by exposure to Agent Orange, simplifying the process for obtaining disability benefits for those conditions.

**Sources:**
¹ [Presumptive Diseases - (presumptive)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#presumptivediseases)  
² [Eligibility - (presumptive)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)  
³ [Eligibility (Part 2) - (eligibility)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Agent Orange](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange)
- [Agent Orange Exposure](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#agentorangeexposure)
- [Presumptive Diseases](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#presumptivediseases)
- [Eligibility (Part 2)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)
- [Eligibility](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 2)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)
- [Vietnam era Veteran eligibility](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)

### Question 130: PRESUMPTIVE-5

**Query:** What is the PACT Act?

**Category:** PRESUMPTIVE

**Response Time:** 4.7s

**Answer:**

The PACT Act, officially named The Sergeant First Class (SFC) Heath Robinson Honoring our Promise to Address Comprehensive Toxics Act, is a law that expands VA health care and benefits for Veterans exposed to burn pits, Agent Orange, and other toxic substances. It is considered one of the largest health care and benefit expansions in VA history. The Act:

- Expands and extends eligibility for VA health care for Veterans with toxic exposures and Veterans of the Vietnam, Gulf War, and post-9/11 eras
- Adds over 20 more presumptive conditions related to burn pits, Agent Orange, and other toxic exposures
- Adds more presumptive-exposure locations for Agent Orange and radiation
- Requires VA to provide a toxic exposure screening to every Veteran enrolled in VA health care
- Helps improve research, staff education, and treatment related to toxic exposures

The PACT Act enables millions of Veterans to access VA health care earlier than previously scheduled and allows Veterans and survivors to file claims for related disability compensation and health care benefits.

**Sources:**
¹ [PACT Act - (presumptive)](https://www.va.gov/resources/the-pact-act-and-your-va-benefits/#pactact)  
² [What’s the PACT Act and how will it affect my VA benefits and care? - (presumptive)](https://www.va.gov/resources/the-pact-act-and-your-va-benefits/#whatsthepactactandhowwillitaffectmyvabenefitsandcare)

**Sources Cited:**
- [PACT Act](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [What’s the PACT Act and how will it affect my VA benefits and care?](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [On this page](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [Am I eligible for VA health care under the PACT Act?](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [Getting benefits](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)

### Question 134: PRESUMPTIVE-9

**Query:** What cancers are presumptive for burn pits?

**Category:** PRESUMPTIVE

**Answer:**

The cancers that are presumptive for burn pits exposure include:

- Brain cancer  
- Gastrointestinal cancer of any type  
- Glioblastoma  
- Head cancer of any type  
- Kidney cancer  
- Lymphoma of any type  
- Melanoma  
- Neck cancer of any type

- Thyroid cancer⁶

### Question 135: PRESUMPTIVE-10

**Query:** Am I eligible for PACT Act benefits?

**Category:** PRESUMPTIVE

**Response Time:** 7.4s

**Answer:**

You may be eligible for PACT Act benefits if you meet the basic service and discharge requirements and any of the following apply to you:

- You served in the Vietnam War, the Gulf War, Iraq, Afghanistan, or any other combat zone after 9/11.
- You deployed in support of the Global War on Terror.
- You were exposed to toxins or other hazards during military service at home or abroad, such as burn pits, sand and dust, particulates, oil well or sulfur fires, chemicals, radiation, warfare agents, depleted uranium, herbicides, and other occupational hazards.

Even if none of these descriptions are true for you, you may still be eligible for VA health care based on your service. The PACT Act expands and extends eligibility for VA health care for Veterans with toxic exposures and Veterans of the Vietnam, Gulf War, and post-9/11 eras, adding more presumptive conditions and exposure locations¹⁴.

You can apply for VA health care or file a disability claim related to the PACT Act now, including filing new claims or Supplemental Claims if your condition is now considered presumptive²⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [Am I eligible for VA health care under the PACT Act?](https://www.va.gov/resources/the-pact-act-and-your-va-benefits/#amieligibleforvahealthcareunderthepactact)

### Question 136: PRESUMPTIVE-11

**Query:** What is toxic exposure?

**Category:** PRESUMPTIVE

**Response Time:** 3.1s

**Answer:**

Toxic exposure refers to coming into contact with hazardous chemicals or other harmful materials during military service. These exposures can include substances like open burn pits, airborne hazards, Agent Orange, radiation, contaminated water, and other related hazards. The VA recognizes certain illnesses or conditions related to these exposures as presumptive conditions for disability compensation.

### Question 138: PRESUMPTIVE-13

**Query:** What is Camp Lejeune water contamination?

**Category:** PRESUMPTIVE

**Response Time:** 2.69s

**Answer:**

Camp Lejeune water contamination refers to the exposure to toxic chemicals found in the drinking water at Marine Corps Base Camp Lejeune and Marine Corps Air Station (MCAS) New River, North Carolina, between August 1, 1953, and December 31, 1987. Veterans, family members, and others who lived, worked, or were otherwise exposed at these locations during this time may be eligible for disability compensation, health care benefits, and other relief due to health conditions linked to this contamination¹.

### Question 140: PRESUMPTIVE-15

**Query:** How do I prove toxic exposure?

**Category:** PRESUMPTIVE

**Response Time:** 5.57s

**Answer:**

To prove toxic exposure for VA disability benefits, you generally need to establish a connection between your military service and your condition. However, for certain health conditions known as "presumptive conditions," the VA automatically assumes that your service caused the condition if you meet specific service requirements. This means you do not have to prove the exposure caused your condition; you only need to show that you meet the service criteria for the presumption¹.

Additionally, the VA offers toxic exposure screenings at VA health facilities. These screenings ask about your potential exposure to hazards such as burn pits, Agent Orange, radiation, and other environmental hazards during your service. The screening can help identify if you have been exposed and inform you about benefits and clinical resources you may need³.

In summary:
- For presumptive conditions, prove you meet the service requirements for the presumption (no need to prove causation)¹.
- You can get a toxic exposure screening at VA health facilities to document potential exposures³.

**Sources:**
¹ [What does it mean to have a presumptive condition for toxic exposure?](https://www.va.gov/resources/the-pact-act-and-your-va-benefits/#whatdoesitmeantohaveapresumptiveconditionfortoxicexposure)  
³ [Toxic exposure screenings](https://www.va.gov/resources/the-pact-act-and-your-va-benefits/#toxicexposurescreenings)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Toxic Exposure and Hazardous Materials](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)

- [What does it mean to have a presumptive condition for toxic exposure?](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)

### Question 141: GIBILL-1

**Query:** What is the GI Bill?

**Category:** GIBILL

**Response Time:** 9.14s

**Answer:**

The GI Bill provides education benefits to veterans through different programs, mainly Chapter 30 (Montgomery GI Bill - Active Duty) and Chapter 33 (Post-9/11 GI Bill).

**Chapter 30 (Montgomery GI Bill - Active Duty):**
- Provides up to 36 months of a monthly stipend.
- Benefits are generally payable for 10 years following release from honorable active service.
- Can be used for college degrees, certificate programs, technical or vocational courses, flight training, apprenticeships, on-the-job training, licensing and certification tests, entrepreneurship training, certain entrance exams, and correspondence courses.
- Includes options like the $600 Buy-Up Program, where servicemembers can contribute up to $600 while on active duty to receive increased monthly benefits.
- May include a "kicker" (Branch College Fund) for veterans in critical shortage MOS, adding up to $950 a month.
- Eligibility requires honorable discharge, a high school diploma or GED (or in some cases 12 college credit hours), and meeting specific service criteria¹⁴⁵.

**Chapter 33 (Post-9/11 GI Bill):**
- Provides up to 36 months of education benefits for service after September 10, 2001.
- Covers full tuition and mandatory fees paid directly to public institutions; for private or foreign schools, tuition and fees are capped at a national maximum rate.
- May include a housing allowance equal to the military BAH for an E-5 with dependents if attending at least one class in a classroom.
- Veterans discharged before January 1, 2013, have 15 years to use this benefit; no time limit if discharged after that date.
- Certain Reservists who lost benefits under REAP may have restored benefits under this program.
- The Supreme Court ruled that veterans who served at least two periods qualifying for both Montgomery GI Bill and Post-9/11 GI Bill may receive additional benefits.
- The GI Bill Comparison Tool can help determine which benefit is best for individual circumstances⁶⁷³.

In summary, the Montgomery GI Bill (Chapter 30) offers a monthly stipend for various education and training programs with a 10-year usage limit, while the Post-9/11 GI Bill (Chapter 33) provides broader education benefits including tuition coverage and housing allowances with more flexible usage periods depending on discharge date.

**Sources:**

### Question 142: GIBILL-2

**Query:** How do I apply for the Post-9/11 GI Bill?

**Category:** GIBILL

**Response Time:** 3.0s

**Answer:**

To apply for the Post-9/11 GI Bill, follow these steps:

- Apply online for Post-9/11 GI Bill benefits through the official VA website.  
- After applying, you will receive a Certificate of Eligibility (COE).  
- Submit your COE to your school’s certifying official (SCO), financial aid office, or military/Veteran liaison. When you do this, be sure to ask about applying for your school’s Yellow Ribbon Program.  
- Your school will determine if they have reached the maximum number of students accepted into the Yellow Ribbon Program and the amount of money you will receive.  
- The school will notify you if you are approved and the amount of benefits you will get.  

These steps ensure your benefits are properly applied to your education program¹.

### Question 143: GIBILL-3

**Query:** What is Chapter 33?

**Category:** GIBILL

**Response Time:** 8.64s

**Answer:**

The Chapter 33 Post-9/11 GI Bill is an education benefit program that provides up to 36 months of education benefits to veterans who have served on Active Duty after September 10, 2001. It can be used for various educational purposes including attending institutions of higher learning, training, licensing, and certification reimbursement. Veterans attending at least one class in a classroom may be entitled to a housing allowance equal to the military Basic Allowance for Housing (BAH) for an E-5 with dependents at the location of the school where the majority of classes are taken.

Eligibility requires an honorable period of Active Duty service on or after September 10, 2001. Veterans discharged before January 1, 2013, have 15 years to use this benefit, while those discharged after that date have no time limit to use it.

The benefits include full tuition and mandatory fees paid directly to public schools, capped tuition and fees for private or foreign schools, a books and supplies stipend up to $1,000 per year (prorated if less than full time), and potential additional payments such as a rural benefit or a kicker for critical shortage MOS enlistments. The rate of pursuit (credit load) affects how quickly the 36 months of benefits are used.

Public colleges and universities must charge veterans and dependents in-state tuition by law when using this benefit and not on Active Duty, although residency requirements may still apply and must be waived.

The program also allows for additional GI Bill benefits if a veteran served qualifying periods under both the Montgomery GI Bill and the Post-9/11 GI Bill.

Overall, the Post-9/11 GI Bill is designed to support veterans in pursuing education and training after their service with comprehensive financial assistance for tuition, housing, books, and other education-related costs³⁴⁵⁶.

³ [Chapter 33 (Post-9/11 GI Bill) (Part 3)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibills#chapter33post911gibi)

### Question 144: GIBILL-4

**Query:** What is the Montgomery GI Bill?

**Category:** GIBILL

**Response Time:** 6.08s

**Answer:**

The Montgomery GI Bill (MGIB) is an education benefit program for veterans who have served on active duty. It provides a monthly stipend for up to 36 months that can be used for various educational and training programs, including college degrees, certificate programs, technical or vocational courses, flight training, apprenticeships, on-the-job training, licensing and certification tests, entrepreneurship training, certain entrance examinations, and correspondence courses. Remedial, deficiency, and refresher courses may also be approved under certain circumstances.

Eligibility generally requires an honorable discharge, a high school diploma or GED (or in some cases 12 hours of college credit), and meeting specific service-related criteria. Benefits are typically payable for 10 years following release from honorable active service.

Additional features include a $600 Buy-Up Program, where servicemembers can contribute extra funds while on active duty to increase their monthly benefits, and a "kicker" or Branch College Fund, which provides additional monthly payments for veterans who joined a military occupational specialty (MOS) with a critical shortage of personnel.

The MGIB can be combined with other GI Bill benefits under certain conditions, such as serving multiple qualifying periods of service.

³⁴⁶

### Question 146: GIBILL-6

**Query:** What is Chapter 30?

**Category:** GIBILL

**Response Time:** 6.79s

**Answer:**

The Chapter 30 Montgomery GI Bill (MGIB) is an education benefit program for veterans who served on active duty. It provides up to 36 months of education benefits that can be used for college degrees, certificate programs, technical or vocational courses, flight training, apprenticeships, on-the-job training, high-tech training, licensing and certification tests, entrepreneurship training, certain entrance examinations, and correspondence courses. Under certain circumstances, remedial, deficiency, and refresher courses may also be approved.

Benefits are generally payable for 10 years following honorable release from active service. Veterans may also participate in a $600 Buy-Up Program to increase their monthly benefits and may receive a "kicker" (additional monthly payment) if they joined a military occupational specialty (MOS) with a critical shortage of personnel.

Eligibility requires an honorable discharge, a high school diploma or GED (or in some cases 12 hours of college credit), and meeting specific service requirements under one of several categories. For example, Category I eligibility includes entering active duty after June 30, 1985, not declining participation, having military pay reduced by $100 a month for the first 12 months, and continuous service for 3 years or 2 years under certain conditions.

Additionally, a recent Supreme Court decision allows veterans who served at least two periods of service—one qualifying for the Montgomery GI Bill and another for the Post-9/11 GI Bill—to potentially receive additional GI Bill benefits.

In summary, the Chapter 30 Montgomery GI Bill is a comprehensive education benefit program for eligible veterans with active duty service, offering financial support for a variety of educational and training opportunities for up to 10 years after discharge³⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Chapter 30 (Montgomery GI Bill (MGIB) - Active Duty) (Part 1) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibills#chapter30montgomeryg)
