### Question 239: PACT-9

**Query:** Can survivors get PACT Act benefits?

**Category:** PACT

**Response Time:** 2.28s

**Answer:**

Yes, survivors of Veterans may be eligible for PACT Act-related benefits. These benefits include:

- A monthly VA Dependency and Indemnity Compensation (DIC) payment for surviving spouses, dependent children, or parents of a Veteran who died from a service-connected disability.
- A one-time accrued benefits payment if the Veteran owed unpaid benefits at the time of their death.
- A Survivors Pension for surviving spouses or children of a Veteran with wartime service.

Survivors can apply for these benefits and may also submit new applications if previous claims were denied but they believe they are now eligible under the PACT Act.

**Sources:**
¹ [The PACT Act and your VA benefits](https://www.va.gov/resources/the-pact-act-and-your-va-benefits/#pactact)  
² [Information for survivors - (presumptive)](https://www.va.gov/resources/the-pact-act-and-your-va-benefits/#informationforsurvivors)

### Question 241: LEJEUNE-1

**Query:** What is Camp Lejeune water contamination?

**Category:** LEJEUNE

**Response Time:** 2.79s

**Answer:**

Camp Lejeune water contamination refers to the exposure to harmful chemicals found in the drinking water at Marine Corps Base Camp Lejeune and Marine Corps Air Station (MCAS) New River, North Carolina, during the period from August 1, 1953, to December 31, 1987. Veterans, family members, and others who lived, worked, or were otherwise exposed at these locations during this time may be eligible for disability compensation, health care benefits, and other relief due to health conditions linked to this exposure¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Camp Lejeune Water Contamination - (presumptive)](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#camplejeunewatercontamination)

### Question 242: LEJEUNE-2

**Query:** Am I eligible for Camp Lejeune benefits?

**Category:** LEJEUNE

**Response Time:** 8.43s

**Answer:**

You may be eligible for Camp Lejeune benefits if you meet certain criteria depending on whether you are a Veteran or a family member:

**For Veterans:**
- You served at Marine Corps Base Camp Lejeune or Marine Corps Air Station (MCAS) New River, North Carolina, for at least 30 days total between August 1, 1953, and December 31, 1987.
- You did not receive a dishonorable discharge when you separated from the military.
- You have a diagnosis of one or more of the following presumptive conditions linked to the contaminated water exposure:
  - Adult leukemia
  - Aplastic anemia and other myelodysplastic syndromes
  - Bladder cancer
  - Kidney cancer
  - Liver cancer
  - Multiple myeloma
  - Non-Hodgkin’s lymphoma
  - Parkinson’s disease¹³⁴

**For family members:**
- You are or have been a family member (birth or adopted child, married spouse, or legal dependent) of a Veteran who served at Camp Lejeune or MCAS New River.
- You lived (or were in utero while your mother lived) at Camp Lejeune or MCAS New River for at least 30 days total between August 1, 1953, and December 31, 1987.
- You apply for and are approved for benefits under current law.
- The health care costs you seek to be paid or reimbursed must be related to one or more of these conditions:
  - Bladder cancer
  - Breast cancer
  - Esophageal cancer
  - Female infertility
  - Hepatic steatosis
  - Kidney cancer
  - Leukemia
  - Lung cancer
  - Miscarriage
  - Multiple myeloma
  - Myelodysplastic syndromes
  - Neurobehavioral effects
  - Non-Hodgkin’s lymphoma
  - Renal toxicity
  - Scleroderma²

If you meet these requirements, you may also be eligible to enroll in VA health care, and if you have any of the 15 covered health conditions, you won’t have to pay a copay for care related to those conditions¹.

To apply for disability compensation, you need to file a claim and provide military records showing your service at Camp Lejeune or MCAS New River for at least 30 days during the specified period and medical records showing a diagnosis of one or more of the presumptive conditions³⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Can I also get health care benefits?](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#canialsogethealthcarebenefits)  
² [Benefits for families who lived at Camp Lejeune](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#benefitsforfamilieswholivedatcamplejeune)  
³ [Am I eligible for VA disability compensation?](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#amieligibleforvadisabilitycompensation)  
⁴ [Camp Lejeune Water Contamination](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#camplejeunewatercontamination)

### Question 243: LEJEUNE-3

**Query:** What conditions are presumptive for Camp Lejeune?

**Category:** LEJEUNE

**Response Time:** 3.0s

**Answer:**

The presumptive conditions for Camp Lejeune, meaning conditions that are presumed to be related to exposure to contaminated water at Camp Lejeune or MCAS New River between August 1, 1953, and December 31, 1987, are:

- Adult leukemia
- Aplastic anemia and other myelodysplastic syndromes
- Bladder cancer
- Kidney cancer
- Liver cancer
- Multiple myeloma
- Non-Hodgkin’s lymphoma
- Parkinson’s disease

You must have a diagnosis of one or more of these conditions to be eligible for presumptive VA disability compensation related to Camp Lejeune water contamination.

### Question 245: LEJEUNE-5

**Query:** Can family members get Camp Lejeune benefits?

**Category:** LEJEUNE

**Response Time:** 2.7s

**Answer:**

Yes, family members who lived at Camp Lejeune or MCAS New River for at least 30 days total between August 1, 1953, and December 31, 1987, may be eligible for VA benefits. To qualify, they must be a birth or adopted child, married spouse, or legal dependent of a Veteran who served at these locations, and they must apply and be approved for benefits under current law. The VA may pay for or reimburse health care costs related to specific conditions linked to the water contamination, such as bladder cancer, breast cancer, leukemia, and others listed by the VA. Family members need to fill out the Camp Lejeune Family Member Program Application (VA Form 10-10068) to apply for these benefits¹.

### Question 246: LEJEUNE-6

**Query:** How do I file a Camp Lejeune claim?

**Category:** LEJEUNE

**Response Time:** 5.26s

**Answer:**

To file a Camp Lejeune claim for disability compensation, follow these steps:

- File a new claim online, by mail, in person, or with the help of a trained professional.  
- When filing, be sure to state that you are applying for one or more of the presumptive conditions related to Camp Lejeune.  
- Provide supporting documents with your claim, including:  
  - Military records showing you served at Camp Lejeune or MCAS New River for at least 30 days total between August 1, 1953, and December 31, 1987.  
  - Medical records showing you have one or more of the 8 Camp Lejeune presumptive conditions.  

You can file online at the VA website or submit VA Form 21-526EZ by mail, fax, or in person at a VA Regional Office. The mailing address for claims is:

```
Department of Veterans Affairs  
Claims Intake Center  
PO Box 4444  
Janesville, WI 53547-4444  
```

You can also get assistance from a Veterans Service Officer (VSO) to help with your claim¹⁶.

If you are filing for relief under the Camp Lejeune Justice Act of 2022 (PACT Act), you or your legal representative can start by filing an administrative claim with the Department of the Navy, but this is separate from VA disability claims².

**Sources:**

¹ [How do I file a claim for disability compensation?](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#howdoifileaclaimfordisabilitycompensation)

⁶ [Filing a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 247: LEJEUNE-7

**Query:** What is the Camp Lejeune Justice Act?

**Category:** LEJEUNE

**Response Time:** 3.21s

**Answer:**

The Camp Lejeune Justice Act of 2022, which is part of Section 804 of the Honoring our Promise to Address Comprehensive Toxics (PACT) Act, allows Veterans, survivors, and families to file for appropriate relief for harm caused by exposure to the contaminated water at Camp Lejeune. This law applies if you lived, worked, or were otherwise exposed at Camp Lejeune for at least 30 days total between August 1, 1953, and December 31, 1987, or if your mother was exposed at Camp Lejeune for at least 30 days during that period while pregnant with you. The Act enables filing for relief under this authority, typically starting with an administrative claim with the Department of the Navy. It is important to note that the PACT Act does not affect VA benefits related to Camp Lejeune but expands VA health care and benefits for Veterans exposed to other toxic substances like burn pits¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [How the PACT Act affects Camp Lejeune claims and related benefits](https://www.va.gov/disability/eligibility/hazardous-materials-exposure/camp-lejeune-water-contamination/#howthepactactaffectscamplejeuneclaimsandrelatedbenefits)

**Sources Cited:**
- [How the PACT Act affects Camp Lejeune claims and related benefits](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [Camp Lejeune Water Contamination](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [On this page](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [Benefits for families who lived at Camp Lejeune](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)

### Question 249: YELLOW-1

**Query:** What is the Yellow Ribbon program?

**Category:** YELLOW

**Response Time:** 3.41s

**Answer:**

The Yellow Ribbon Program is a provision related to the Post-9/11 GI Bill that helps cover tuition and fee costs that exceed the standard benefits. It is a program where schools voluntarily enter into an agreement with the VA to fund tuition expenses that are not covered by the GI Bill. The amount you can receive depends on factors set by the school, such as degree type and program. To participate, you must apply for Post-9/11 GI Bill benefits, submit your certificate of eligibility to your school, and request to apply for the Yellow Ribbon Program. The school then determines if you are accepted and the amount of funding you will receive³⁴⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Reference - (education)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#reference)  
² [Yellow Ribbon Program - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#faq)  
³ [Yellow Ribbon Program - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#faq)  
⁴ [Yellow Ribbon Program - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#faq)  
⁶ [Application Process - (education)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#applicationprocess)

### Question 251: YELLOW-3

**Query:** How does Yellow Ribbon work with GI Bill?

**Category:** YELLOW

**Response Time:** 4.71s

**Answer:**

The Yellow Ribbon Program is specifically associated with the Post-9/11 GI Bill (Chapter 33) and not with the Montgomery GI Bill (Chapter 30). Here is how it works with the Post-9/11 GI Bill:

- The Post-9/11 GI Bill provides up to 36 months of education benefits, including full tuition and mandatory fees for public institutions.
- For private or foreign schools, tuition and fees are capped at a national maximum rate.
- Veterans attending private higher learning institutions may be eligible for the Yellow Ribbon Program, which helps cover additional education-related costs not covered by VA.
- To use the Yellow Ribbon Program, you must apply for it through your school’s certifying official or financial aid office when you submit your Certificate of Eligibility (COE).
- The school will determine if they have reached the maximum number of students accepted into the Yellow Ribbon Program and the amount of money you will receive.
- The school will notify you if you are approved and the amount of Yellow Ribbon funding you will get.

The Montgomery GI Bill (Chapter 30) does not mention eligibility or use of the Yellow Ribbon Program in the provided context.

Therefore, Yellow Ribbon is a supplementary program to the Post-9/11 GI Bill to help cover tuition costs exceeding the VA cap at private or foreign schools, but it does not apply to the Montgomery GI Bill benefits⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Application Process - (education)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#applicationprocess)  
² [Chapter 33 (Post-9/11 GI Bill) (Part 3) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibills#chapter33post911gibi)  
⁶ [Chapter 30 (Montgomery GI Bill (MGIB) - Active Duty) - (education)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibill#chapter30montgomerygibillmgibactiveduty)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Application Process](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#applicationprocess)

- [GI Bill Education Benefits - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/gibill#faq)

### Question 254: YELLOW-6

**Query:** How do I apply for Yellow Ribbon?

**Category:** YELLOW

**Response Time:** 4.34s

**Answer:**

To apply for the Yellow Ribbon Program, follow these steps:

1. Apply for Post-9/11 GI Bill benefits through the official VA application process.  
2. Submit your Certificate of Eligibility (COE) to your school, typically to the school’s certifying official (SCO), financial aid office, or military/Veteran liaison. When you submit your COE, be sure to ask to apply for your school’s Yellow Ribbon Program.  
3. Wait for your school to determine if they have available spots in the program and the amount of money you will receive.  
4. Your school will notify you if you are approved and the amount of Yellow Ribbon funding you will receive.  

This process ensures your participation in the Yellow Ribbon Program if your school offers it and you meet their criteria¹.

**Sources:**  
¹ [Application Process - (education)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/yellow#applicationprocess)

### Question 255: PENSION-1

**Query:** What is Aid and Attendance?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 4.03s

**Answer:**

Aid and Attendance is a benefit that may be awarded to veterans who require the regular aid and attendance of another person due to their disabilities. It is available as an increased VA Pension for veterans with non-service related conditions or as Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) for those with service-connected conditions.

Specifically, Aid and Attendance under SMC L is awarded to veterans who meet one or more of the following criteria:

- Anatomical loss or loss of use of both feet;
- Anatomical loss or loss of use of one hand and one foot;
- Loss of sight or blindness in both eyes;
- Permanently bedridden; or
- Need daily help with basic needs such as eating, dressing, and bathing.

Additionally, a veteran may qualify if their disability (mental or physical) requires that another person regularly helps to keep them from harming themselves or others.

An important policy note is that the veteran's service-connected disabilities that justify the need for Aid and Attendance generally must be from a single disease process that combines to 100% or has an evaluation of 100%. If this is not met, an advisory opinion from VA Central Office is required, which may be unfavorable, and denial may require appeal to the Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA)¹³.

Aid and Attendance is distinct from other related benefits such as Special Aid and Attendance (SMC R), which has additional criteria related to the level of care and supervision required daily by the veteran.

**Sources:**
¹ [Aid and Attendance (Pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#aidandattendancepension)  
³ [SMC L (Aid and Attendance)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/smc#smclaidandattendance)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Aid and Attendance (Pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#aidandattendancepension)
- [Aid and Attendance (Pension) - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#references)
- [SMC L (Aid and Attendance)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/smc#smclaidandattendance)

- [Housebound - Special Monthly Pension - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-housebound#references)

### Question 256: PENSION-2

**Query:** Am I eligible for Aid and Attendance?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 3.49s

**Answer:**

To be eligible for Aid and Attendance (A&A) you must:

- Qualify for VA Pension; **AND**
- Meet at least one of the following criteria:
  - Need another person to help you perform daily activities, like bathing, feeding, and dressing;
  - Have to stay in bed or spend a large portion of the day in bed because of illness;
  - Are a patient in a nursing home due to loss of mental or physical abilities related to a disability;
  - Eyesight is limited to 5/200 or less in both eyes, or concentric contraction of the visual field to 5 degrees or less, even with glasses or contacts¹.

Additionally, for service-connected disabilities, Aid and Attendance is awarded if the veteran meets one of the following:

- Anatomical loss or loss of use of both feet;
- Anatomical loss or loss of use of one hand and one foot;
- Loss of sight or blindness in both eyes;
- Permanently bedridden;
- Need daily help with basic needs like eating, dressing, and bathing;
- Disability requires another person regularly helps to keep from harming self or others³.

If you meet the pension eligibility and one or more of these criteria, you may be eligible for Aid and Attendance.

**Sources:**
¹ [Eligibility - Aid and Attendance (Pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-aa#eligibility)

### Question 257: PENSION-3

**Query:** What is housebound status?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 5.46s

**Answer:**

Housebound status refers to a condition where a veteran is substantially confined to their dwelling and immediate premises due to service-connected disabilities. This confinement must be reasonably certain to continue throughout the veteran's lifetime. Veterans who are housebound are either:

- Permanently confined to their home or, if institutionalized, to their ward or clinical areas due to service-connected disabilities (Housebound In-Fact).

- Or meet the statutory requirements, which include having a single 100% disability rating and a separate 60% rating, or multiple ratings that combine to at least 60% (Statutory Housebound).

The veteran's confinement does not exclude occasional trips for medical appointments or other necessary activities like attending places of worship. Additionally, the veteran should have service-connected disabilities that combine to at least 100% for the in-fact housebound status.

Housebound status can result in eligibility for Special Monthly Compensation (SMC S) or increased VA Pension benefits, depending on whether the condition is service-connected or non-service-connected.

**Sources:**
¹ [Housebound - Special Monthly Pension - (pension)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-housebound#houseboundspecialmonthlypension)  
² [SMC S (Housebound)/(Statutory Housebound) - (compensation)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/smc#smcshouseboundstatutoryhousebound)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Housebound - Special Monthly Pension](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-housebound#houseboundspecialmonthlypension)
- [SMC S (Housebound)/(Statutory Housebound)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/smc#smcshouseboundstatutoryhousebound)
- [Housebound - Special Monthly Pension - Legal References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pension-housebound#references)

### Question 258: PENSION-4

**Query:** What is Special Monthly Pension?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 3.12s

**Answer:**

Special Monthly Pension (SMP) is an increased VA Pension benefit available to veterans who are housebound or need aid and attendance due to non-service related conditions. Veterans who are housebound as a result of non-service related conditions may qualify for this increased pension benefit. For veterans whose housebound status or need for aid and attendance is due to service-related conditions, they may qualify for Special Monthly Compensation (SMC), which is a different benefit related to service-connected disabilities¹⁴.

In summary:
- SMP applies to veterans housebound due to non-service related conditions.
- Aid and attendance needs due to non-service related conditions may also qualify for increased VA Pension.
- For service-connected conditions causing housebound status or aid and attendance needs, Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) is applicable instead¹⁴.

### Question 261: PENSION-7

**Query:** Can I get Aid and Attendance for a nursing home?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 5.48s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can qualify for Aid and Attendance (A&A) benefits if you are in a nursing home, provided you meet the eligibility criteria for Aid and Attendance. Aid and Attendance is awarded to veterans who need daily help with basic needs such as eating, dressing, and bathing, or who are permanently bedridden, among other criteria¹. 

For veterans who need aid and attendance due to non-service related conditions, they may qualify for an increased VA Pension that includes Aid and Attendance benefits². For those whose need for aid and attendance is due to service-connected disabilities, they may qualify for Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) at levels such as SMC L or SMC R1/R2, which also cover aid and attendance³⁶.

Additionally, VA nursing home care is available for veterans enrolled in the VA health system who need nursing home care for service-connected disabilities or who have a 70% or higher service-connected disability. For others, eligibility for nursing home care depends on income and pension status⁵.

In summary, Aid and Attendance benefits can apply to veterans in nursing homes if they meet the criteria related to needing assistance with daily living or are bedridden, and the benefit can be part of an increased pension or special monthly compensation depending on service connection and disability rating.

### Question 262: PENSION-8

**Query:** What is VA pension?

**Category:** PENSION

**Response Time:** 2.28s

**Answer:**

VA Pension is a monetary benefit provided by the Department of Veterans Affairs for veterans who are unable to qualify for VA disability compensation or for those whose monetary benefit is greater via pension. It is a tax-free monthly payment intended to help veterans with limited income who are permanently disabled and spend most of their time at home due to that disability. 

Additionally, there are increased pension benefits available for veterans who need aid and attendance due to non-service related conditions, and special monthly compensation options for those with service-related conditions requiring aid and attendance⁷.

### Question 263: DTA-1

**Query:** What is VA's duty to assist?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 5.87s

**Answer:**

The VA's duty to assist is the obligation of the Department of Veterans Affairs to help veterans obtain the benefits and recognition they deserve by assisting in two main areas:

- Obtaining relevant records necessary to support a claim.
- Providing a medical examination or obtaining medical opinions when required.

Additionally, the duty to assist is preceded by the VA's duty to notify the veteran, which involves sending a foundational notice (known as a 5103 notice) explaining what evidence is needed to properly support the claim or appeal. Failure to send this notice is a common error related to the duty to assist.

The VA will provide assistance even if the claim is vague or incomplete by seeking clarification or informing the claimant of missing information.

However, the VA's duty to assist with providing a medical examination or medical opinion only applies if all three of the following elements are met:

1. A statement of symptoms or current diagnosis.
2. An in-service event or a service-connected disability that caused or worsened the condition.
3. A reasonable possibility that the condition is related to service (nexus).

If these elements are not all met, the VA has no duty to provide an exam or medical opinion. Also, no new exam is required for supplemental claims if no new and relevant evidence is submitted, and if the maximum schedular rating can be granted without an exam, one is not needed³⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Duty to Assist (DTA) - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#dutytoassistdta)  
² [Standard Procedures - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#standardprocedures)  
³ [Duty to Assist (DTA) - Legal References - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#references)  
⁶ [Providing a Medical Examination/Obtaining Medical Opinions - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#providingamedicalexaminationobtainingmedicalopinions)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 264: DTA-2

**Query:** What is VA's duty to notify?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 2.27s

**Answer:**

The VA's duty to notify is a foundational notice requirement where the VA must inform claimants about the evidence needed to properly support their claim or appeal. This notice is commonly known as the "5103 notice." It explains what information and evidence the claimant needs to provide to substantiate their claim. Failure to send this notice is considered a common error in the VA's duty to assist. The duty to notify ensures that veterans are aware of the necessary steps and documentation required for their claims to be processed effectively¹³.

### Question 265: DTA-3

**Query:** Does the VA have to get my records?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 4.45s

**Answer:**

The VA has a duty to assist veterans by making reasonable efforts to obtain relevant records related to the claimed disabilities that will reasonably further the claim. This includes military, federal, private medical, employment, and other records. Typically, VA will make about two attempts to obtain these records. However, the ultimate responsibility to supply records lies with the veteran. If the VA is unable to obtain relevant records, they must notify the veteran about what records could not be obtained, the attempts made, and any further actions VA will take.

For private medical records or Vet Center records, the veteran must provide a signed release form (VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a) for the VA to obtain them. The VA will not pay fees to obtain records, and if a private provider charges for copies, the VA will cancel the request and notify the veteran that it is now their responsibility to provide those records.

If the veteran fails to respond timely (within 30 days) to requests for necessary details or release forms, the VA may decide the claim without further attempts to get those records.

In summary:
- VA will make reasonable efforts (usually 2 attempts) to obtain relevant records.
- Veteran must provide necessary release forms for private records.
- Veteran is ultimately responsible for supplying records if VA cannot obtain them.
- VA will notify the veteran if records cannot be obtained despite efforts.
- VA will not pay fees for obtaining records.
- Failure to respond timely may result in VA making a decision without further attempts.

Therefore, while the VA does have a duty to assist and will try to get your records, it is ultimately your responsibility to provide them if the VA cannot obtain them after reasonable efforts¹³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Obtaining Records - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#obtainingrecords)  
³ [Obtaining/Types of Evidence - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Obtaining Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#obtainingrecords)

### Question 266: DTA-4

**Query:** Can I complain if VA didn't help get my records?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 3.44s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can raise a complaint or concern if the VA did not help you obtain your records. The VA is required to make reasonable efforts (usually two attempts) to obtain relevant records related to your claimed disabilities that will reasonably further your claim. However, it is ultimately the Veteran's responsibility to supply these records to the VA. If the VA is unable to obtain relevant records, they must notify you about:

- What records they could not get;
- Explanation of attempts made to get the records;
- Explanation that further efforts would be futile or the records do not exist;
- Description of any further action the VA will take.

If the VA fails to meet these obligations, you can seek assistance or file a complaint through a Veterans Service Organization (VSO) or at a VA regional office where a VA employee can help you¹ ⁷.

**Sources:**
¹ [Obtaining Records - (claims_process)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/dta#obtainingrecords)  
⁷ [Getting Help - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/survivorbenefits#top)

### Question 267: DTA-5

**Query:** What records will the VA request for me?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 4.52s

**Answer:**

The VA will make reasonable efforts to obtain relevant records that relate to your claimed disabilities and will reasonably further your claim. These records commonly fall into five categories:

- Military records (medical and personnel)
- Federal (non-military) records
- Private medical records
- Employment (non-federal) records
- Other relevant records

The VA will attempt to obtain these records, usually making two attempts. However, the Veteran is ultimately responsible for providing these records if the VA is unable to obtain them. If the VA cannot obtain certain records, they will notify you about which records could not be obtained, the attempts made, and any further actions they will take¹.

For private medical records and Vet Center records, you must provide the VA with a signed release form (VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a) for the VA to request those records on your behalf⁴⁵.

Additionally, the VA has a duty to assist and will request Service Treatment Records (STRs) for you if you are unable to obtain them yourself⁵.

### Question 268: DTA-6

**Query:** What is a duty to assist error?

**Category:** DTA

**Response Time:** 2.04s

**Answer:**

A duty to assist error occurs when the VA fails to fulfill its obligation to help Veterans obtain the evidence needed to support their claim or appeal. One common example of a duty to assist error is the failure to send the foundational notice known as the "5103 notice," which explains what evidence is required to properly support the claim or appeal¹.

### Question 269: EDGE-1

**Query:** Can I get VA benefits with a bad conduct discharge?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.71s

**Answer:**

You may still be eligible for VA benefits with a bad conduct discharge, depending on the specifics of your discharge characterization. According to the eligibility criteria:

- A character of discharge that is Honorable, General, or Under Honorable generally qualifies for VA disability benefits.
- Even if your character of discharge was not Honorable, you may still be eligible for benefits¹.

However, if your discharge was dishonorable, you are generally not eligible for VA benefits². Additionally, if your condition is the result of wilful misconduct or occurred during a period of dishonorable conduct, the VA will not grant service connection for that condition³.

In summary:
- Bad conduct discharge (which is different from dishonorable discharge) may still allow eligibility for VA benefits.
- Dishonorable discharge typically disqualifies you from VA benefits.
- Conditions resulting from wilful misconduct or during dishonorable conduct are not service-connected.

### Question 271: EDGE-3

**Query:** Can I get benefits with an other than honorable discharge?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 5.63s

**Answer:**

Whether you can get benefits with an Other than Honorable (OTH) discharge depends on the specific benefit and circumstances:

- For GI Bill education benefits, if your GI Bill entitlement was earned during a period of honorable service, you can keep using it even if you later receive an OTH discharge from the Reserves/National Guard¹. Also, if you reenlisted or had an earlier honorable period of service, you may still qualify for the GI Bill, though benefits might be prorated if the honorable service period was not long enough to earn full entitlement².

- For VA disability benefits, generally, your character of discharge must be Honorable (including General, Under Honorable) to be eligible⁴ ⁷. However, even if your discharge was not Honorable, you may still be eligible in some cases, especially if you have a mental health diagnosis and apply for a discharge upgrade³ ⁴.

- Some benefits explicitly cannot be granted based on certain disability claims (like 1151 grants), but this does not directly relate to discharge status⁵.

In summary, an OTH discharge can limit eligibility for many VA benefits, but exceptions exist, especially if you have prior honorable service or pursue a discharge upgrade.

### Question 272: EDGE-4

**Query:** What if I was dishonorably discharged?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.81s

**Answer:**

If you were dishonorably discharged, the VA will **not** grant service connection for any condition that is a direct result of wilful misconduct or occurred during a period of dishonorable conduct. This includes conditions established while in AWOL or deserter status, drunk driving incidents, or other misconduct examples listed. Essentially, a dishonorable discharge generally bars you from VA benefits related to service connection for disabilities caused by misconduct.

Additionally, eligibility for VA benefits requires that the veteran's character of discharge was **not** dishonorable. Certain crimes and circumstances can also make a veteran ineligible, including federal or state capital crimes and sex offender convictions with life imprisonment sentences⁵.

Regarding education benefits like the GI Bill, a dishonorable discharge typically disqualifies you from benefits unless you had a prior honorable period of service or reenlistment that qualifies you. In such cases, benefits may be prorated based on the time served honorably⁴⁶.

### Question 273: EDGE-5

**Query:** Can reservists get VA disability?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.01s

**Answer:**

Yes, reservists can get VA disability compensation if they incur or aggravate an injury, illness, or disease while in the line of duty. This includes periods of Active Duty for Training (ADT), Inactive Duty Training (IADT), drill weekends, or any other training while on orders, as well as traveling directly to or from their place of duty. To properly file a disability claim, reservists should have their unit issue a Line of Duty (LOD) determination or a Notice of Eligibility (NOE) if in the U.S. Coast Guard Reserve. If they do not obtain an LOD/NOE, they may rely on buddy letters or personal statements as evidence. Additionally, having pre- and post-deployment physicals can serve as excellent evidence for claims, provided the information was honestly reported during those exams¹⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [National Guard/Reserves - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim#top)

### Question 274: EDGE-6

**Query:** Can National Guard get VA benefits?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 5.48s

**Answer:**

Yes, National Guard members can get VA disability benefits if they incur or aggravate an injury, illness, or disease while in the line of duty, such as during Active Duty for Training (ADT), Inactive Duty Training (IADT), drill weekends, or any other training while on orders. This coverage also includes traveling directly to or from their place of duty. To properly file a disability claim, it is important to have a Line of Duty (LOD) determination issued by their unit. Without an LOD, they may need to rely on buddy letters or personal statements as evidence. Pre- and post-deployment physicals can also serve as useful evidence if accurate and complete¹.

Additionally, eligibility for VA disability benefits requires having a chronic disability resulting from military service or a preexisting condition worsened by service, an honorable or general under honorable discharge, and service on Active Duty, ADT, or IADT, which includes National Guard service².

### Question 275: EDGE-7

**Query:** What is character of discharge?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.26s

**Answer:**

The "character of discharge" refers to the classification or characterization of a veteran's separation from military service. It indicates the nature of the discharge, such as Honorable, Other than Honorable, or other types, which can affect eligibility for VA benefits. For veterans with an Other than Honorable discharge and a mental health diagnosis, it is recommended to apply for an upgrade of their character of discharge to potentially improve their benefits eligibility¹.

### Question 276: EDGE-8

**Query:** Can I get VA benefits if I never deployed?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.08s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get VA benefits even if you never deployed. The basic eligibility requirements for VA disability benefits include:

- Having a chronic disability that was the result of your military service, a preexisting condition made worse by your military service, or a result of an already service-connected disability.
- Having an Honorable or General Under Honorable discharge.
- Serving on Active Duty (AD), Active Duty for training (ADT), or Inactive Duty Training (IADT).

Deployment is not explicitly required for eligibility as long as these conditions are met¹.

### Question 277: EDGE-9

**Query:** What if my condition started after service?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 4.43s

**Answer:**

If your condition started after service, it may still be possible to get service connection if your service-connected condition aggravated an existing post-service condition. To establish this, the VA must determine:

- The level of severity of the condition before aggravation; and  
- The level of severity after aggravation.

You need to provide medical evidence showing the severity before aggravation or as soon as possible after aggravation but before the current severity is established. The VA will then obtain a medical opinion to decide if your service-connected condition worsened the post-service condition beyond its natural progression. If the opinion is favorable, you can be granted service connection by aggravation.

If the condition is not related to aggravation of a service-connected condition and started after service, the context does not provide specific information about direct service connection for conditions starting after service.

**Sources:**  
¹ [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)  
² [Service-Connection by Aggravation](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#serviceconnectionbyaggravation)

### Question 278: EDGE-10

**Query:** Can I get benefits for conditions that existed before service?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.11s

**Answer:**

Generally, conditions that existed before service, such as congenital or genetic conditions, are not rated for VA benefits. These are conditions you were born with, like male pattern baldness, missing or extra body parts, personality disorders, or poor eyesight. However, there are exceptions:

- If the condition manifests during your service after the time period medical science has determined it should have developed, it can be rated.
- If the condition worsened due to your service or a service-connected condition (aggravated), it can be rated.
- For Active Duty service members who serve at least 8 years (applies to DoD Medboards only), there may be considerations.

So, while pre-existing conditions generally are not rated, if they worsen or manifest during service in a way recognized by VA, you may be eligible for benefits related to those conditions¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Congenital or Genetic Conditions - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/norate)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/nehmer#nehmerclassmembers38cfr3816)

### Question 279: EDGE-11

**Query:** What is pre-existing condition aggravation?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 6.18s

**Answer:**

Pre-existing condition aggravation refers to the worsening of a condition that existed before military service due to that service. Specifically, it involves a preexisting (pre-service) condition that is made worse by military service, which can lead to service-connection for the aggravated condition.

Key points about pre-existing condition aggravation include:

- The Veteran must have had the condition before service, which is typically documented during the Military Entrance Processing Station (MEPS) physical examination. If the condition was not recorded at that time, the VA presumes the Veteran was sound (had no condition) at entry, unless exceptions apply.

- Exceptions to the presumption of soundness include chronic conditions diagnosed shortly after service entry that likely existed before service, or medical evidence showing the condition existed prior to service (e.g., medical records or in-service x-rays showing healed fractures).

- Statements by the Veteran or others about the condition existing before service have no weight unless supported by medical evidence.

- If the Department of Defense lost the entrance physical records, the VA assumes no preexisting condition unless exceptions apply.

- To establish aggravation, the VA must determine the severity of the condition before and after aggravation and obtain a medical opinion that the service-connected condition worsened the preexisting condition beyond its natural progression.

- When rating an aggravated preexisting condition, the VA subtracts the preexisting condition's severity from the current severity to determine the aggravated rating. If the preexisting condition would rate 100%, the final aggravated rating will be 100%³.

In summary, pre-existing condition aggravation is when military service worsens a condition that existed before service, and the VA can grant service connection for the increased severity caused by service under specific evidentiary and rating rules.

**Sources:**

¹ [Aggravation of a Preexisting (Pre-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofapreexistingpreservicecondition)  
² [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)  
³ [Rating Aggravated Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#ratingaggravatedconditions)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Aggravation of an Existing (Post-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofanexistingpostservicecondition)
- [Aggravation of a Preexisting (Pre-service) Condition](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agg#aggravationofapreexistingpreservicecondition)

### Question 280: EDGE-12

**Query:** Can I get benefits for genetic conditions?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.19s

**Answer:**

Generally, congenital or genetic conditions (those you were born with) are not eligible for VA disability ratings. However, there are exceptions where such conditions can be rated:

- If the condition manifests during your service after the time period medical science has determined it should have developed.
- If the condition worsens due to your service or a service-connected condition (this is called aggravation).
- For Active Duty service members who serve at least 8 years, this applies only to DoD Medboards.

So, you may be able to get benefits for a genetic condition if it meets one of these exceptions¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Congenital or Genetic Conditions - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/norate)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Congenital or Genetic Conditions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/norate)

### Question 281: EDGE-13

**Query:** What if I can't remember my stressor?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.59s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.0%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

If you can't remember your stressor, the VA process allows for some flexibility in how stressors are claimed and verified. Specifically:

- Stressors do not require a specific claim form and can be researched based on information found in VA Form 21-0781, lay statements, military records, or medical records/statements made to an examiner.
- In some cases, such as combat or fear-based stressors, examinations can be ordered without specific stressor details.
- However, if the examination results in a PTSD diagnosis, the VA will verify that the stressor associated with the diagnosis is consistent with the places and circumstances of your service.

This means that even if you cannot recall the exact stressor, the VA may still proceed with an examination and use available evidence to verify the stressor related to your PTSD claim¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [3.  Concession of an In-Service Stressor (Part 3) - (claims_process)](Not available)

### Question 282: EDGE-14

**Query:** Can I file a claim without medical records?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 4.75s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **49.9%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

You can file a claim without having medical records in hand, but there are important considerations:

- You must have the bare minimum evidence before filing a claim, which includes:
  1. A statement of symptoms or a current diagnosis.
  2. An in-service event or a service-connected disability that caused or worsened your condition.
  3. A nexus (a layperson would think the symptoms and the in-service event are related).  
  If you do not have all three parts, your claim may be denied without even being sent to a Compensation & Pension Exam (C&P)¹.

- If you do not have private medical records or non-VA hospital reports, you can provide VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a to authorize the VA to obtain those records for you².

- The VA can access your VHA medical records themselves, so it is not necessary to upload them yourself when filing a claim³.

- You do not need to have a diagnosis to claim something when filing a Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) claim⁴.

In summary, while you can file a claim without medical records, you should at least provide a statement of symptoms, evidence of an in-service event, and a nexus. You can also authorize the VA to obtain your medical records for you.

**Sources:**
¹ [BARE MINIMUM You Need BEFORE You File a Claim:](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Miscellaneous Evidence - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
³ [Filing a Claim - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁴ [Filing a BDD Claim - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 283: EDGE-15

**Query:** What if my doctor won't write a nexus letter?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.08s

**Answer:**

If your doctor won't write a nexus letter, it may be due to their personal or professional policy or because they want to be properly compensated for their time and effort. You should make it clear that you will compensate them appropriately, as writing a nexus letter involves reviewing your records, doing relevant research, and typing up the opinion, which can cost at least a thousand dollars or more. If they still refuse, you will need to seek out another physician who is willing to provide the nexus letter¹.

### Question 284: EDGE-16

**Query:** Can I get benefits for conditions not in my service record?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.51s

**Answer:**

You generally need to have a condition that is linked to your military service to receive VA disability benefits. Specifically, your claim must include:

- A statement of symptoms or current diagnosis.
- An in-service event or service-connected disability that caused or worsened your condition.
- A nexus, meaning a reasonable connection between the condition and the service event or disability¹.

If your condition is not documented in your service records, it may be difficult to establish service connection unless you can provide lay statements or other evidence supporting that the condition started or was aggravated during service¹.

Additionally, if you have medical records from outside military treatment facilities, these are not automatically included in your service treatment records (STRs). You must coordinate with your base medical to have those private records added to your STRs for your claim to be considered complete⁷.

In summary, benefits for conditions not in your service record are possible only if you can establish the condition's connection to your service through other evidence such as lay statements or private medical records properly added to your service records.

**Sources:**
¹ [BARE MINIMUM You Need BEFORE You File a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁷ [Active Duty Servicemembers](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 285: EDGE-17

**Query:** What is lay evidence?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.1s

**Answer:**

Lay evidence is testimony or statements provided by a person who is not a medical professional but has firsthand knowledge about a veteran's condition or circumstances. It is used to help establish service incurrence or aggravation of a condition, especially when service treatment records are unavailable. For combat veterans or former prisoners of war (FPOWs), lay evidence is acceptable if it is satisfactory, consistent with the circumstances of combat or internment, and can reasonably be expected to be within the knowledge of a layperson¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [2. Lay Evidence (Part 1) - (claims_evidence)](https://www.knowva.ebenefits.va.gov/system/templates/selfservice/va_ssnew/help/customer/locale/en-US/portal/554400000001018/content/554400000014389/M21-1-Part-V-Subpart-ii-Chapter-1-Section-B-Reviewing-Testimony-and-Lay-Evidence#2a)

### Question 289: NEHMER-1

**Query:** What is the Nehmer rule?

**Category:** NEHMER

**Response Time:** 4.42s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.6%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

The Nehmer rule refers to a VA policy under 38 CFR 3.816 that requires the VA to grant earlier effective dates for service connection (SC) for certain conditions that become presumptive for Vietnam Veterans exposed to Agent Orange. Specifically, when a new presumptive condition is added to the regulation, the VA must review prior denied claims and grant earlier effective dates if there was evidence of the condition before it became presumptive. This means that veterans do not need to file a new claim for an earlier effective date if their prior claim was denied before the condition was recognized as presumptive, as long as the claim was filed before the presumptive date and there is evidence of diagnosis. However, Nehmer applies only to conditions named in 38 CFR 3.309(e) except chloracne, and does not apply to claims filed after the condition became presumptive or to conditions not yet added to the regulation. Additionally, some conditions like hypertension and MGUS are not eligible for Nehmer consideration. Claims filed after the condition became presumptive are still eligible for liberalizing law consideration but not Nehmer.

**Sources:**
¹ [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 4) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)  
² [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 3) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 4)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 3)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)

### Question 290: NEHMER-2

**Query:** Who is a Nehmer class member?

**Category:** NEHMER

**Response Time:** 4.46s

**Answer:**

A Nehmer class member is a veteran who was exposed to Agent Orange in Vietnam or its waters (including waterways and ports, such as those served by the Brown and Blue Water Navy) and who developed a covered condition listed in 38 CFR 3.309(e) (excluding chloracne), or their survivor who claimed entitlement to a related death or survivor benefit, and whose claim was denied between September 25, 1985, and the date before the condition became presumptive. Additionally, if any claim or appeal was decided from September 25, 1985, onward and there was medical evidence of a diagnosis of a condition that later became presumptive—even if that condition was not at issue in the claim or appeal—the veteran is eligible for Nehmer consideration. Veterans exposed to Agent Orange outside Vietnam or its waters are not eligible for Nehmer benefits³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 2) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)  
² [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 1) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)  
³ [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 4) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 4)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 2)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 3)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)

### Question 291: NEHMER-3

**Query:** Can I get an earlier effective date under Nehmer?

**Category:** NEHMER

**Response Time:** 7.71s

**Answer:**

You may be eligible for an earlier effective date under the Nehmer class action if you meet certain criteria:

- You are a Veteran exposed to Agent Orange who developed a covered condition, or a survivor claiming entitlement to a related death or survivor benefit.
- Your claim for the covered condition was denied between September 25, 1985, and the date before the condition became presumptive for Agent Orange exposure.
- The condition is one of those named in 38 CFR 3.309(e), excluding chloracne, hypertension, and MGUS (which are not eligible under Nehmer).
- The claim was initiated or entitlement arose before the condition became presumptive.
- If your claim was received within a year of separation, the backdate can be to the date after separation or date entitlement arose, whichever is later.

If these conditions are met, the VA is required to backdate the effective date of your claim to the date it was initiated or entitlement arose, whichever is later. This backdating is not limited by the liberalizing law rules, and the appeal does not need to be continuously pursued. Additionally, if the condition was later granted on appeal through any basis (secondary, direct, different presumptive), the VA can reopen the issue and award an earlier effective date under 38 CFR 3.816.

Claims or appeals do not need to have originally named Agent Orange as the basis of the condition to qualify for Nehmer benefits.

Examples include:
- A Veteran whose claim for lung cancer was initially denied before the condition became presumptive can have the effective date backdated to the original claim date.
- A Veteran who filed a claim for coronary artery disease before it became presumptive can have the effective date backdated to the earlier claim date if medical evidence showed diagnosis at that time.

However, if your original claim was denied before September 25, 1985, Nehmer does not apply, though liberalizing law may still be considered.

In summary, if your situation fits these criteria, you can get an earlier effective date under Nehmer³⁴⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)  
² [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 4)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)  
³ [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) - (claims_special)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/nehmer#nehmerclassmembers38cfr3816)  
⁴ [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 3)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)  
⁵ [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) - (presumptive)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38cfr3816)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 4)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/nehmer#nehmerclassmembers38cfr3816)

### Question 293: NEHMER-5

**Query:** Does Nehmer apply to Blue Water Navy veterans?

**Category:** NEHMER

**Response Time:** 2.08s

**Answer:**

Yes, Nehmer applies to Blue Water Navy veterans, but only if their Agent Orange exposure happened in Vietnam or its waters, which includes waterways and ports where Blue Water Navy veterans served. Veterans exposed in other countries are not eligible for Nehmer benefits. This means Blue Water Navy veterans who served in the waters of Vietnam are subject to Nehmer for eligible conditions¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 2) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)

### Question 295: PT-1

**Query:** What is Permanent and Total disability?

**Category:** PT

**Response Time:** 4.15s

**Answer:**

Permanent and Total (P&T) disability is a special status granted to veterans that signifies they are paid at the 100% disability rate (total) and their disabilities are considered static (permanent). However, the term "permanent" is somewhat misleading because it does not mean the disability rating cannot be reduced. The VA generally cannot reduce a rating if it has been held for at least 20 years, except in cases of fraud. Veterans with P&T status do not have regularly scheduled reexaminations unless specific conditions apply. The decision letter for P&T does not explicitly state the status but indicates entitlement to Dependents Educational Assistance (DEA). Veterans can be P&T even if their combined rating is under 100%, such as through Total Disability Individual Unemployability (TDIU). P&T veterans can work unless they have work restrictions due to TDIU⁵⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - (rating_info)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#permanentandtotalptdisability)  
² [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
⁵ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
⁶ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)

### Question 296: PT-2

**Query:** How do I know if I'm P&T?

**Category:** PT

**Response Time:** 3.18s

**Answer:**

To know if you are considered Permanent and Total (P&T) disabled by the VA, you can check your VA Benefit Summary letter on the official VA website:

- Log in to your account at [va.gov](http://va.gov/).
- Click the green box labeled “Get your VA benefit letters.”
- Select “View letters,” then “VA Letters,” and finally open the “Benefit Summary and Service Verification Letter.”
- Scroll down to the section that states: “You **are** considered to be totally and permanently disabled due solely to your service-connected disabilities.”
- If it says you are, then you have P&T status; if it says you aren't, then you do not have P&T status.

This is the official way to verify your P&T status according to VA records¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)

### Question 297: PT-3

**Query:** What benefits come with P&T status?

**Category:** PT

**Response Time:** 3.03s

**Answer:**

The benefits that come with Permanent and Total (P&T) status include:

- Entitlement to Chapter 35 Dependents Educational Assistance (DEA) for the veteran's dependents.
- Removal of the 10% penalty for early withdrawal of 401K and IRAs.
- Eligibility for the veteran's dependents to enroll in CHAMPVA (Civilian Health and Medical Program of the Department of Veterans Affairs).
- During a medical emergency, the veteran may freely use non-VA emergency rooms for any condition, with the requirement to submit a claim within 2 years for reimbursement.
- Expedited processing of the veteran's disability claims by Social Security.
- Free air travel on Space-A for the veteran and their dependents, with travel allowed within the Continental United States and certain U.S. territories.

These benefits provide educational, financial, healthcare, social security, and travel advantages to veterans with P&T status and their dependents¹.

**Answer:**

### Question 299: PT-5

**Query:** What is statutory P&T?

**Category:** PT

**Response Time:** 3.21s

**Answer:**

Statutory P&T (Permanent and Total) refers to certain service-connected disabilities that are automatically considered permanently and totally disabling by law. Veterans with these specific disabilities are granted P&T status without the need for further evaluation of permanence or totality. The disabilities that qualify for statutory P&T include:

- Loss of the use of both feet;
- Loss of both hands;
- Loss of both eyes;
- Loss of one foot and one hand;
- Loss of one foot and one eye;
- Loss of one hand and one eye;
- Loss of hearing of both ears;
- Organic loss of speech; or
- Being permanently helpless or permanently bedridden.

Veterans with these disabilities are also entitled to Special Monthly Compensation (SMC).

Additionally, active pulmonary tuberculosis is considered permanently and totally disabling for Non-service-connected pension purposes if certain severe conditions or progression criteria are met, such as involvement beyond the respiratory system, severe symptoms, extensive cavity formation, or lack of improvement after prolonged hospitalization¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Statutory P&T - (rating_info)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#statutorypt)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Statutory P&T](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#statutorypt)

- [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)
- [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)

### Question 300: PT-6

**Query:** How do I become P&T?

**Category:** PT

**Response Time:** 6.69s

**Answer:**

To become Permanent and Total (P&T) disabled, you must have a combined disability rating of 100% or be granted Total Disability based on Individual Unemployability (TDIU), and the conditions that make up this 100% evaluation or TDIU grant must be static (not expected to improve). Even if you have some non-static conditions, you can still be P&T as long as those non-static conditions do not factor into the 100% rating or TDIU grant¹⁵⁷.

If you have been rated 100% combined or TDIU and your conditions are static but were not awarded P&T, the VA usually conducts an automatic audit within about two weeks of the rating decision to grant P&T if appropriate. If no audit occurs, you can take action by:

- Making a VERA appointment and requesting a supervisor to address the missed issue,
- Filing a Higher Level Review within one year citing the missed issue of dependents’ educational assistance,
- Or filing a claim specifically for entitlement to Dependent’s Educational Assistance due to being permanent and total.

A rater will then review your file and grant P&T if appropriate⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - (rating_info)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#permanentandtotalptdisability)  
⁴ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
⁵ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
⁷ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ - (faq)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)

### Question 301: PT-7

**Query:** Is TDIU the same as P&T?

**Category:** PT

**Response Time:** 5.25s

**Answer:**

TDIU (Total Disability based on Individual Unemployability) and P&T (Permanent and Total) are related but not exactly the same.

- P&T is a special status where a Veteran is paid at the 100% disability rate and their disabilities are considered static (permanent). However, the term "permanent" is somewhat misleading because the VA can still reduce the evaluation except in certain cases (such as holding the evaluation for 20 years)¹⁴.
- TDIU is a grant for Veterans who are unable to hold gainful employment due to their service-connected disabilities, which may result in a 100% disability rating for compensation purposes⁵.
- A Veteran can be rated under 100% and still be considered P&T if they have a TDIU grant³.
- Veterans who are P&T can work unless their P&T status is based on TDIU, in which case there are work restrictions².
- P&T status means no regularly scheduled reexaminations, but Veterans with TDIU P&T are still bound by income limits¹.

In summary, TDIU can be part of a P&T rating, but they are not exactly the same. P&T refers to the overall status of being permanently rated at 100%, while TDIU is a specific type of 100% rating based on unemployability.

**Sources:**
¹ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
² [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
³ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#faq)  
⁴ [Permanent and Total (P&T) Disability - (rating_info)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt#permanentandtotalptdisability)  
⁵ [Unemployability - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)

### Question 302: PT-8

**Query:** Does P&T mean I never have future exams?

**Category:** PT

**Response Time:** 2.18s

**Answer:**

No, having a Permanent and Total (P&T) disability status does not mean you will never have future exams. Veterans who are P&T do not have regularly scheduled reexaminations, but this does not mean they are completely exempt from all future exams. There can be exceptions where exams or reevaluations may still occur, especially if certain conditions change or new claims are filed.

### Question 304: BWN-2

**Query:** Am I a Blue Water Navy veteran?

**Category:** BWN

**Response Time:** 3.96s

**Answer:**

You are considered a Blue Water Navy veteran if you served offshore of the Republic of Vietnam, including within 12 nautical miles seaward of a specific line starting at the southwestern demarcation line of the waters of Vietnam and Cambodia, between January 9, 1962, and May 7, 1975. This includes service on U.S. Navy or Coast Guard vessels operating in those offshore waters during that period.

If you are unsure whether your vessel traveled within these eligible offshore waters, VA has a special team to research military and service treatment records to determine your status. You are encouraged to file a claim and provide any information about your service aboard a U.S. military vessel that operated less than 12 nautical miles from the demarcation line during the specified dates. VA will review your claim and determine if you qualify as a Blue Water Navy veteran³.

**Sources:**
¹ Blue Water Navy Vietnam Veterans Act (Part 2) - (presumptive)  
² Blue Water Navy Vietnam Veterans Act (Part 1) - (presumptive)  
³ Blue Water Navy Vietnam Veterans Act (Part 4) - (presumptive)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Blue Water Navy Vietnam Veterans Act (Part 2)]()
- [Blue Water Navy Vietnam Veterans Act (Part 1)]()
- [Blue Water Navy Vietnam Veterans Act (Part 4)]()

- [Agent Orange Exposure - FAQ](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#faq)
- [Eligibility (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)

### Question 305: BWN-3

**Query:** What conditions are presumptive for Blue Water Navy?

**Category:** BWN

**Response Time:** 4.26s

**Answer:**

The Blue Water Navy Vietnam Veterans Act of 2019 presumes that Veterans who served offshore of the Republic of Vietnam between January 9, 1962, and May 7, 1975, were exposed to herbicides such as Agent Orange. Veterans meeting this service criteria may be entitled to service connection for conditions related to that exposure.

While the exact list of presumptive conditions for Blue Water Navy Veterans is not fully detailed in the provided context, it is noted that there are fourteen diseases currently on the presumptive list for Agent Orange exposure. These include:

- Amyloid light-chain (AL) amyloidosis  
- Chloracne, or other acneform disease consistent with chloracne  
- All Chronic B-cell leukemias (including hairy-cell leukemia and chronic lymphocytic leukemia)  
- Diabetes mellitus, Type 2  
- Lymphoma, Hodgkin’s disease, and others (the list is truncated in the source)  

These conditions are associated with herbicide exposure such as Agent Orange, which Blue Water Navy Veterans are now presumed to have encountered under the Act.

**Sources:**
¹ [Blue Water Navy Vietnam Veterans Act (Part 1) - (presumptive)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/presumptive)  
² [Blue Water Navy Vietnam Veterans Act (Part 2) - (presumptive)]

### Question 307: BWN-5

**Query:** How far offshore counts as Blue Water Navy?

**Category:** BWN

### Question 310: BURN-2

**Query:** What conditions are presumptive for burn pit exposure?

**Category:** BURN

**Response Time:** 5.77s

**Answer:**

The presumptive conditions for burn pit exposure include both cancers and respiratory illnesses.

**Presumptive Cancers:**
- Brain cancer
- Gastrointestinal cancer of any type
- Glioblastoma
- Head cancer of any type
- Kidney cancer
- Lymphoma of any type
- Melanoma
- Neck cancer of any type
- Pancreatic cancer
- Reproductive cancer of any type
- Respiratory (breathing-related) cancer of any type¹

**Presumptive Respiratory and Related Illnesses:**
- Asthma diagnosed after service
- Chronic bronchitis
- Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD)
- Chronic rhinitis
- Chronic sinusitis
- Constrictive bronchiolitis or obliterative bronchiolitis
- Emphysema
- Granulomatous disease
- Interstitial lung disease (ILD)
- Pleuritis
- Pulmonary fibrosis
- Sarcoidosis¹

Additionally, pulmonary emphysema and interstitial lung diseases are specifically noted as presumptive if exposed to burn pits²³.

### Question 313: BURN-5

**Query:** What cancers are presumptive for burn pits?

**Category:** BURN

**Response Time:** 5.53s

**Answer:**

The cancers that are presumptive for burn pits exposure include:

- Brain cancer  
- Gastrointestinal cancer of any type  
- Glioblastoma  
- Head cancer of any type  
- Kidney cancer  
- Lymphoma of any type  
- Melanoma  
- Neck cancer of any type  
- Pancreatic cancer  
- Reproductive cancer of any type  
- Respiratory (breathing-related) cancer of any type  

Additionally, specific cancers listed under various diagnostic codes are also presumptive if exposed to burn pits:

- Liver cancer¹  
- Bile duct, colon, esophagus, gall bladder, pancreas, liver, salivary gland, small intestine, or stomach cancers¹  
- Ovary cancer²  
- Thyroid cancer⁶  
- Lung, bronchus, larynx, trachea, and breathing-related cancers⁷  

These cancers are recognized as presumptive conditions for veterans exposed to burn pits, meaning service connection is presumed for these cancers if the veteran was exposed to burn pits during service³⁴⁵⁶⁷.

### Question 317: MENTAL-1

**Query:** How is PTSD rated?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 4.96s

**Answer:**

PTSD (Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder) is rated under the general mental disorder ratings system, which bases the rating on the severity of the Veteran's total symptoms rather than the specific diagnosis. PTSD is not rated differently from other mental health disorders in terms of the rating criteria, but it does have unique criteria for service-connection, requiring an in-service stressor and a separate examination (DBQ) from other mental health conditions.

The rating for PTSD depends on the level of occupational and social impairment caused by the symptoms. For example, a 50% rating corresponds to occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as panic attacks more than once a week, difficulty understanding complex commands, impaired judgment, and disturbances of motivation and mood. The Veteran may be mostly able to care for themselves but may have difficulty maintaining employment and social relationships².

Additionally, PTSD symptoms can overlap with Traumatic Brain Injury (TBI) symptoms. The VA may lump the ratings together unless the examiner can clearly separate the symptoms of PTSD and TBI, in which case separate ratings can be assigned⁴.

In summary:
- PTSD is rated like other mental disorders based on symptom severity.
- Service-connection for PTSD requires an in-service stressor.
- Ratings reflect occupational and social impairment from symptoms.
- PTSD ratings can be combined or separated from TBI ratings depending on symptom differentiation.

**Sources:**
¹ [Mental Disorder Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [PTSD Service-Connection](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
³ [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
⁴ [PTSD & TBI - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Mental Disorder Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [PTSD Service-Connection](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [50%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [PTSD & TBI](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [TBI Rating Example](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/TBI)
- [Rating Schedule for The Central Nervous System](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [General Rating Formula for Mental Disorders](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental#generalratingformulaformentaldisorders)

### Question 318: MENTAL-2

**Query:** What are the criteria for a 70% mental health rating?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 2.79s

**Answer:**

The criteria for a 70% mental health rating include occupational and social impairment with deficiencies in most areas such as work, school, family relations, judgment, thinking, or mood. Symptoms that may be present include:

- Suicidal ideation
- Obsessional rituals which interfere with routine activities
- Speech intermittently illogical, obscure, or irrelevant
- Near-continuous panic or depression affecting the ability to function independently, appropriately, and effectively
- Impaired impulse control (such as unprovoked irritability with periods of violence)
- Spatial disorientation
- Neglect of personal appearance and hygiene
- Difficulty in adapting to stressful circumstances (including work or a worklike setting)
- Inability to establish and maintain effective relationships

These symptoms result in significant occupational and social impairment¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [General Rating Formula for Mental Disorders](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental#generalratingformulaformentaldisorders)

### Question 319: MENTAL-3

**Query:** What is occupational and social impairment?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 3.93s

**Answer:**

Occupational and social impairment refers to the degree to which a mental disorder affects a person's ability to function effectively in work, school, family relations, judgment, thinking, mood, and social interactions. It ranges from mild impairment with transient symptoms that decrease work efficiency only during significant stress, to total impairment with gross disturbances in thought processes, communication, and behavior.

For example:
- At a 10% rating level, occupational and social impairment involves mild or transient symptoms that decrease work efficiency and ability to perform tasks only during periods of significant stress, with the individual usually able to care for themselves and rarely having problems working or socially¹.
- At a 100% rating level, total occupational and social impairment is characterized by gross impairment in thought processes or communication, persistent delusions or hallucinations, inappropriate behavior, danger to self or others, inability to perform daily living activities, disorientation, and severe memory loss, resulting in an inability to work or maintain social relationships²³.

**Sources:**
¹ [10%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [General Rating Formula for Mental Disorders](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental#generalratingformulaformentaldisorders)  
³ [100%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

**Sources Cited:**
- [General Rating Formula for Mental Disorders](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental#generalratingformulaformentaldisorders)
- [10%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [100%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
